Credit std Project Summary
Borrower:                 Government of Kenya
Beneficiary:              Kenya Agricultural Research Institute
Amount:                   S1R 15.5 million (US$19.6 million equivalnt)
Terms:                    Standard IDA terms; proceeds would be passed on as
a grant to KARI
Project Objectives:       The proposed project is designed to: (i) focus
agricultural research on priority research
activities .ich have a significant likelihood of
early returns; (ii) improve the quality and cost
effectiveness of research to generate technology
pdrticulary for smallholdings; (iii) intensify
producer participation in research program
formulation; (iv) strengthen linkages with other
agricultural services, especially extension; and
(v) improve cooperation with other agricultural
research institutions. The strategy to achieve
these objectives would be to assist the Government
of Kenya (GOK) in reorganizing the various
agricultural research establishments into a single
Kenya Agricultural Research Institute (KARI) and
to support KARI in the implementation of the
National Agricultural Research Project (NARP).
The project is a five-year time slice of a long-
term agricultural research development plan and a
key element of GOK's investment program in
agriculture.
Project Description:      The project would be supported by several donors
and would comprise assistance for: (i)
restructuring of KARI organization and management,
including establishment costs, construction of a
headquarters building, training and external
expert assistance for research planning and
management, budgeting and information systems;
(ii) rehabilitation and provision of equipment and
operating funds to Regional Research Centers which
undertake adaptive research; and (iii) support to
key national crop, livestock, factor and
disciplinary research programs. IDA would provide
funding for incremental non-salary operating costs
(on a declining basis), vehicles, equipment, civil
This document has a restricted distribution and may be used by recipients only in the performance
of their official duties. Its contents may not otherwise be disclosed without World Bank authorization.



(ii)
works, the research information and reporting
system, and some training and consultancy
services. Effective donor coordination is an
essential element of the project.
Benefits and Risks:       The project is expected to bring about much
greater efficiency of resource utilization in
agricultural research. Benefits arising from
improyed focus of reseach priorities, higher
quality research and greater effectiveness of
research/extension linkages would show up in
KARI's impact on agricultural production. The
project would directly contribute to technology
development, thereby sustaining and accelerating
agricultural productivity and growth. The
critical role of future productivity increases
derives from the necessity to cope with the
challenges of Kenya's growing population and the
need to ensure competitiveness and diversification
in agricultural exports. The project's four main
risks relate to: (i) effectiveness of the
reorganized KARI organization and management; (ii)
adequacy of budgetary funding levels and
mechanisms; (iii) adherence to agreed priorities
in research programs and capacity to make sound
judgements as necessary; and (iv) relevance and/or
adoption of research findings. The project
includes specific measures to minimize the
potentially adverse impact of such risks. It also
includes detailed annual programming mechanisms
and periodic research reviews involving scientists
outside KARI. In addition, supervision,
monitoring and evaluation would be conducted on a
joint donor/GOK basis, and an in-depth review is
scheduled after the first 3 years of the project.

preparation documents, several joint-donor reports and the findings of an
IDA appraisal mission in February/March 1987. Mission members included

I. THE AGRICULTURAL SECTOR; IMPLICATIONS FOR RESEARCH
A. Agriculture in Kenya's Economy
1.01     The Kenya Agricultural Sector Report of 1986 provides a detailed
account and analysis of Kenya's agricultural sector characteristics,
objectives, performance and constraints.l/ Despite progress in
industrialization, the agricultural sector remains vitally important for
the continued growth and development of the domestic economy.
Agriculture's share of the gross domestic product (31% in 1985) remains
high. The population is predominantly rural (85Z) and agriculture provides
employment (directly or indirectly) for nearly 70% of the nation's people.
The sector produces almost all the nation's food and serves as the main
source of export earnings (682 in 1986) and raw materials for the
predominantly resource-based industrial sector. Ranches and large farms
account for about 401 of total farmed area and smallholders co-er the
remaining 60%; the proportion of land owned and operated by smallholders
will increase with the continuing subdivision of large and group-owned
farms. The average size of Kenya's 2 million (M) smallholdings is about
2.3 ha (1985). Smallholder agriculture accounts for approximately 752 of
production and for 85% of agricultural employment; smallholder-oriented
agricultural research is therefore crucial to increased production.
B. Agricultural Performance and Near Term Prospects
1.02     The first decade after Independence showed vigorous agricultural
growth (4.7% per annum). However, this slowed to an average 3.2% between
1972 and 1983, while population growth accelerated to 3.9%, signalling a
trend towards decline in per capita production. Most agricultural growth
in the past 20 years has come about as a result of:
(i)     expansion in land area cultivated;
(ii)    improved producer prices;
(iii)   increased availability of farm inputs; and
(iv)    farm inv3stment in livestock, tree crops, equipment, land
improvement, irrigation and other infrastructure.
During much of the past decade, Government of Kenya (GOK) investment in
agricultural extension, research and animal health services declined, there
were problems in fertilizer distribution and agricultural labor use
stagnated. However, GOK has recently made a serious effort to bring about
improvements in particular in extension and animal health services (for
details see Annex I) and also through the Agricultural Sector Adjustment
Program (para. 1.08, 1.09).
1.03     In order for the agricultural sector to help meet Kenya's
development targets, the sources of agricultural growth must change
because: (i) continued expansion in cultivated land area is severely
1/ Kenya Agricultural Sector Report No. 4629-KE; January 1986.



-2-
constrained (para. 1.04); and (ii) greater production incentives are
difficult to provide now that prices for main commodities have reached
approximate world price parity levels (para. 1.06). Analysis undertaken by
IDA for an Agricultural Sector Adjustment Program indicated that without
significant agricultural policy changes and the widespread introduction of
improved technology, agricultural growth is unlikely to keep up with
population growth.2/    It is therefore essential for Kenya to shift to a
strategy of improving agricultural productivity through better agricultural
research, improved extension and more effective and efficient animal health
services. However, farm investment (private and public) should be
encouraged simultaneously and the supply of farm inputs and fertilizer is
also to be expanded. The policy changes required for this are embodied in
the Kenya Agricultural Sector Adjustment Program, wnich became effective
March 30, 1987 and which forms a framework and policy umbrella for
individual projects such as the proposed National Agricultural Research
Project (NARP).
C. Major Constraints and Policy Issues
1.04     A major constraint for agricultural development in Kenya is the
shortage of arable land, exacerbated by rapid population growth.  Only
about 20Z of Kenya's 575,000 km2 surface area is good quality land; another
92 can produce crops but is subject to periodic drought and the remaining
land area is suitable mainly for extensive livestock production. Other
major land-related problems are soil erosion and degradation, forest
depletion and the slow subdivision of large farms. kenya is only gradually
developing a coherent land policy and an institutional framework for
addressing land resource issues. Because of these various constraints,
future agricultural growth will have to come mainly from yield improvements
per unit area of land.
1.05     Although input use in high potential areas and for crops such as
coffee, tea and sugar is relatively high, use of fertilizer and also of
improved seeds for major food crops has virtually stagnated in the last
decade. Consumption of fertilizer has been constrained by: (i) low
financial returns to farmers using fertilizer on cereal food crops; (ii)
insufficient farmers know-how due in part to lack of site-specific
recommendations; and (iii) chronic fertilizer shortages caused by
restrictive import quotas, late price and allocation announcements and poor
coordination of donor-provided fertilizers (late delivery, inappropriate
fertilizer types and distribution problems). There is a lack of new
varieties suitable for the mixed and intercropping systems practiced by
small farmers. Recently, seed supply has been negatively affected by
deteriorating margins for producers, difficulties in funding the National
Seed Quality Control Service and incipient management and financial
problems of the Kenya Seed Company.
1.06     Prior to 1982, official prices for major domestic commodities
(maize, wheat, milk, meat, sugarcane and cotton) lagged behind world prices
and the agricultural sector terms of trade deteriorated relative to the
rest of the economy. Producer prices for export crops however, were
determined by world markets and have been high by African standards.
2/ President's Report No. P4278-KE; June, 1986.



-3-
Beginning in 1982, GOK significantly increased official prices for major
food crops to provide greater incentives for farmers. With adequate
prices, the major problem in this area now is marketing. Government has
restricted participation in marketing and processing for commodities such
as maize, wheat and milk, to parastatal marketing enterprises. Of
particular importance is the National Cereals and Produce Board which plays
the central role in assuring food security, but is troubled by mounting
financial losses. Poor performance of these institutions has emerged as a
significant constraint to the provision of marketing and processing
services, food security, farmer incentives, credit supply, and public
finance. Nearly 502 of the Ministry of Agriculture's (MOA) budget is taken
tiy agricultural parastatals, pre-empting funds which could otherwise be
used to provide essential extension and research services. For other crops
such as fruits, flowers and vegetables which can be freely marketed, the
private sector and cooperatives have been hampered in developing marketing
channels by lack of credit, administrative regulation of imports (seeds,
chemicals) and difficulties in product exports.
1.07     Capital formation in the agricultural sector has declined from
2.52 of GDP in 1978 to 1.452 in 1983. Returns to public sector investment
have mostly been low because of poorly conceived projects and weak project
management. Public sector agricultural credit schemes reach few
smallholder families; out of some 2 million farm households, only 20,000
(mainly medium and large scale farmers), receive credit from the
Agricultural Finance Corporacion. This Corporation has severe
institutional problems: a poor quality portfolio, unclear objectives and
low recovery rates. Private banks concentrate on short-term lending to the
large farm sector. Term lending in agriculture dropped to 252 of total
term lending to the economy in 1983 (against an average of 35? in the
19708). Private investment has been discouraged by low returns and legal
barriers to entry in processing and marketing.
D. The Agricultural Sector Adlustment Program
1.08     In 1985, GOK decided to deal comprehensively with the above
constraints to agricultural development and preparation of an agricultural
adjustment program was initiated with International Development Association
(IDA) assistance.3/      The measures being taken in the context of the
Credit and Special African Facility for the adjustment program (Cr. 1717-
KE), are at the core of GOK's development strategy; their implementation
would create an environment in which investments to improve agricultural
services would be more rewarding and where well-designed and conducted
agricultural research could have a much greater effect on productivity.
Specifically, they include:
(i)     increased fertilizer imports;
(ii)    improved fertilizer policy (pricing, marketing,
distribution);
3/ For details see President's Report No. P4278-KE; June, 1986.



-4-
(iii)   adjustment in policies for (and  .creased supply of) other
inputs (seeds, chemicals and machinery);
(iv)    expansion in credit availability through policy reform;
(v)     higher producer prices and improved agric.ltural marketing
systems including a greater role for the private sector;
(vi)    greater Government budget allocations to agriculture with
expenditures on research and extension to be significantly
increased in real terms; and
(vii)   more efficient investment in agriculture through the
budget rationalization program, improved sector management
and aid coordination.
1.09     Some progress has already been made in implementation of the
adjustment program with policy improvements being generally introduced on
schedule. includings
*i)     beginning reform of the maize marketing system and the
National Cereals and Produce Board with an expanded role
for the private sector;
(ii)    improvements in fertilizer marketing, import licensing and
planning, resulting in increased and more timely supplies;
(iii)   plans to improve fertilizer pricing policy;
(iv)    maintenance of producer prices at appropriate incentive
levels (appropriately adjusted world prices);
(v)     the launching of important reforms in the Agriculture
Finance Corporation;
(vi)    preparation of a strategy and action plan for
restructuring selected State Corporations in the
agriculture sector; and
(vii)   solid progress in budget rationalization.
E. Bank StrateRy and Experience in the Sector
1.10     The Bank Group's strategy for assisting Kenya's agricultural
development coincides with Government objectives. It is growth-oriented
with a primary emphasis on the intensification of production, particularly
of smallholders, which offers much untapped potential and meets
complementary goals of income distribution as well as employment and
foreign exchange generation.   The Bank's lending program consists of two
complementary components: (i) policy-oriented lending (e.g. the adjustment
program; and (ii) project lending. The impact of the latter is being
enhanced by the former and includes projects to: improve essential farmer
services (especially research, extension, animal health, input supply,



-5-
marketing and credit); address key physical resource management issues
(forestry and soil conservation); support better policy planning and
implementation; assist in restructuring public investment; improve training
for agricultural staff; and promote aid coordination.
1.11     To date, the Bank Group has financed over 30 projects in Kenya
(agriculture, forestry and fisheries). The present portfolio of ten on-
going projects reflects substantial pruning and a redirection of effort
toward strategic priorities. Future project lending will directly
complement the Government's sector adjustment program, providing integrated
support for longer-term institution building and development of improved
agricultural and farmer services. The pipeline includes in addition to
agricultural research, proposals in the areas of forestry, semi-arid land
development, agricultural extension phase II, agricultural sector
adjustment phase II, credit, export crops, and small-scale irrigation.  The
practical experience gained through project implementation has been
complemented by considerable sector work. In addition to the 1986
Agricultural Sector Report, studies have been done on agricult'lral inputs,
credit, maize marketing and pricing, farmer incentives, irrigation, land
issues, cooperatives, the public investment program, resource allocation,
private sector investment, and non-government organizations. Studies are
underway on coffee and forestry. These studies provide the basis for a
continuation of Bank Group lending including further elaboration of policy
reforms.
1.12     Recent Project Completion Reports and Project Performance Audit
Reports for agricultural projects in Kenya have provided many
recommendations. Two lessons are particularly relevant for the proposed
National Agricultural Research Project. Firstly, project design must
account for Government's limited institutional and management capacity.
This has been done in the case of NARP where much of the preparation effort
(2 years) was devoted to developing a workable and efficient organization
and management system (paras. 5.01-5.17). Secondly, project design must
account for the impact of frequently conflicting donor demands. In the
case of NARP, donor coordination has been rigorously pursued (para. 5.33,
5.34).
1.13     In addition to these general lessons, the World Bank Group has
learned from its involvement in agricultural research components of several
projects: (coffee research in the Smallholder Coffee Project (CR 914-RE);
the Tea Factory Project (Ln 993-KE), now closed; sugar research in the
Sugar Rehabilitation Project (Ln 1636-KE), now closed; cotton research in
the Cotton Processing and Marketing Project (Cr 1237-KE); and arid lands
agricultural research in the Baringo Semi-Arid Areas Pilot Project (Cr 962-
KE).
1.14     Evaluation reports for several completed projects and supervision
reports for the ongoing projects show that most research components
performed poorly due to: (i) weaknesses in project design; (ii) shortages
of management expertise; and (iii) inadequate Government budget
allocations. Most important however, was the lack cf an overall
agricultural research strategy backed by a well-managed agricultural
research organization. The NARP was developed specifically to address
these problems and all agricultural research presently financed by the Bank
Group and other donors, would be consolidated into the NARP.



II. AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH; PAST AND FUTURE
A. Background to the Proposed Research Program
His tory
2.01     Agricultural research in Kenya started early this century in
response to needs as viewed by the colonial administration and the
settlers' farming community. The greatest expansion of the research
network took place between 1940 and 1955 when a large number of national
and regional research stations were established under the Department of
Agriculture (see Annex I for details). Research establishments were also
set up under what was later to become the East African Community (EAC).
Soon after Independence, it became clear that the sharing of responsibility
for research by a large number of independent research institutes and the
Department of Agriculture's Scientific Research Division (SRD) led to
fragmented and ineffective programs. GOK approved the Science and
Technology Act in 1977 (amended in 1979), to facilitate the creation of
statutory research institutes and a Kenya Agricultural Research Institute
(KARI) was established in 1979. This Institute inherited from the EAC the
facilities at Muguga with research programs on animal health, animal
produrtion, forestry, crops, factors (e.g. soil and water management) and
disciplines (e.g. plant protection); KARI was also intended to coordinate
all agricultural research carried out by Government Departments. KARI was
however, never developed as envisaged due to interdepartmental disagreement
and there was no improvement in the management of research.
Current Research Organization
2.02     At the Project preparation stage, the Ministry of Agriculture and
Livestock Development managed most agricultural research. In November
1986, this Ministry was divided into the Ministry of Agriculture (MOA) and
the Ministry of Livestock Development (MOLD), with responsibility for
research assigned to the Scientific Research Division of MOA and the
Veterinary Research Division (VRD) of MOLD. Although a large part of
agricultural research was under SRD and VRD, institutional responsibility
was also assigned to KARI, Research Foundations (e.g. for coffee and tea),
the Agricultural Faculties of the Universities (Nairobi and Egerton) and
several private sector enterprises such as East African Industries
(oilseeds), the Delmonte Company (fruits), flower and vegetable export
organizations, the Kenya Seed Company as well as sisal, tobacco and sugar
companies. Related research was carried out by the Ministry of Environment
and Natural Resources in forestry (including agro-forestry), wildlife,
range management and fisheries and by the Ministry of Water Development in
hydrology and irrigation.   Thus, the fragmentation of research
responsibility persisted while provisions for linkage and coordination were
entirely inadequate.



-7-
Maior Constraints
2.03     GOK has made significant investments in agricultural research and
several donors, including IDA, have assisted the research program.
External support to research mostly consisted of funding adaptive research
components as part of larger projects (e.g. for rural development).
Despite these efforts, the performance of Kenya's agricultural research
institutions deteriorated during the past decade. This was due to several
serious problems in Kenya's agricultural research system (for details see
Annex I). The constraints have been both institutional (lack of effective
management systems) and structural (inappropriate research orientation and
inadequate long-term program formulation); the major ones were:
-       A proliferation of small donor-financed projects, each
with different management systems, research priorities and
objectives, resulting in duplication between centers and
programs and inefficient use of technical assistance.
This was permitted by the lack of coordinated priority
setting in research and the fractured nature of
agricultural research institutions.
The absence of comprehensive management systems to ensure
the appropriate allocation and effective utilization of
all available research resources and facilities,
exacerbated by insufficient delegation of responsibility
for the design and the implementation of research programs
for key commodities, factors and disciplines. Procedures
for internal research management to supervise, monitor and
evaluate research programs and research staff need
improvement. Information dissemination is inadequate.
Due to the lack of a rational policy for the establishment
of agricultural research facilities, recruitment and
deployment o' staff has not been related to research
needs. Although the total number of research staff is
high, only a limited number have the required skills. In
addition, inadequate career development opportunities and
training has caused scientists to loose motivation
resulting in too frequent turnover of staff. The
proportion of the operating budget for agricultural
research needed to pay staff (76? in 1985) is too high,
with an insufficient budget for the execution of programs
and for the maintenance of research infrastructure.
Linkages are weak and improved contacts are required
within the parent institutions (MOA and MOLD) in
particular with extension but also with input cupply,
credit and animal health; and with outside institutions
such as the Universities, International Agricultural
Research Centers (IARCs) and the private sector.



- 8 -
Finally, the present approach to research program
formulation has failed to adequately recognize the site-
specific demands for new technologies by extension,
farmers and private enterprise.
B. Preparation of the Proposed Project
2.04     To deal with these problems, GOK decided in 1984 to develop a new
research strategy, to prepare a proposal to consolidate the several
agricultural research institutions and numerous projects into one single
manageable institute and to set long-term priorities for research (paras.
5.08- 5.10). The International Service for National Agricultural Research
(ISNAR) was invited to prepare an agricultural research subsector report.
GOK, in 1986 restated its policies on agricultural research in the
Sessional Paper No. 1, "Economic Management for renewed Growth"; in 1987,
GOK reformulated its agricultural research strategy (see below). The ISNAR
report (December, 1985) identified major issues facing agricultural
research and recommended numerous solutions. Following these
recommendations, Government organized a task force supported by ISNAR and
Bank staff, to prepare a detailed outline of actions that would need to be
taken to revitalize the Kenya agricultural research system. In May 1986,
this work culminated in a draft report entitled 'Kenya National Agricultural
Research Project which describes: (i) the long-term Kenya Agricultural
Research Program; and (ii) the immediate requirements for implementing the
first phase (five years) of this program (i.e. the proposed Project). A
joint-donor pre-appraisal mission including Australia, Canada, the European
Economic Community (EEC), the Food and Agriculture Organization, the United
Nations Development Program, the Federal Republic of Germany, ISNAR,
Sweden, the Netherlands, the Overseas Development Administration of the
United Kingdom (ODA), and the United States Agency for International
Development (USAID), led by IDA, reviewed this draft and provided
recommendations for improvement. These recommendations were incorporated
and the NARP report was formally issued in September, 1986. Another joint-
donor mission was organized by IDA in October 1986, to evaluate this
proposal. This mission supported NARP as a blueprint for change, prepared
an evaluation report and expressed long-term donor support for the
implementation of the Program. An earlier appraised project for support to
NARP components by USAID became effective in December, 1986. During early
1987, several other donors organized their own missions to appraise NARP
components for assistance; an IDA mission visited Kenya in February/March,
1987 to appraise the entire NARP and to determine the IA-supported
project.
C. The National Agricultural Research Strategy
Approach
2.05     Kenya requires significant increases in agricultural production to
keep pace with population growth, provide additional employment, generate a
better standard of living and increase exports. There is great pressure on
the resource base of land and water. Increased agricultural productivity
which at the same time maintains or improves the resource base is therefore
essential. The great diversity in topography and rainfall (Map IBRD 20114)



- 9 -
as well as agroclimates (Map IBRD 20116) necessitates a strengthened
regional adaptive research capacity. In order to meet these unprecedented
challenges, a more efficient and productive agricultural research system is
urgently needed in addition to improvements in other agricultural services
(para. 1.08). To achieve these goals, three broad strategies operating
within a constraint of limited available land of high production potential,
will be pursued. First, farmers would be encouraged to use more productive
practices: improved marketing policies and extension will be the main
instruments to obtain higher yields with known technologies. Second,
research would be reorganized and reoriented to fariers' needs to generate
appropriate new technologies. Third, where possible, production patterns
would be diversified to crops that generate higher returns and increased
employment.
2.06     Agricultural research is critical to the realization of important
national goals, i.n particular the objective of ensuring sufficient food for
a rapidly growing population. Agricultural research can only meet the
demands of farmers and the agricultural industry by sharply fccused
research programs addressing priority problems. This calls for a more
effective research structure and efficient use of available research
resources as well as rehabilitation and improvement of infrastructure and
strengthening of research management. All of these are prerequisites for
the projected needed growth of agriculture of 4.5 percent per annum during
the next two decades. The major goals for agricultural research will be
to: focus research on the national goals of economic growth and respond to
the challenges of future socio-economic development; improve the quality
and efficiency of research output; increase productivity in particular of
small holdings; and strengthen critical linkages especially with extension
and related research institutes.
2.07     The strategy to achieve these goals will include: clear definition
of national research priorities and long-term planning of key research
programs; appropriate training and manpower employment; rationalization of
research center activities and mandates; effective coordination of research
programs; improved research support services; achievement of stability and
continuity in research funding; strengthened research-extension linkages;
increased participation by producers in research activities; and
establishment of effective links with other national and international
research centers. The institutional restructuring of the research system
by integrating the various research activities into one national
organization for agricultural research would precede the implementation of
these strategies. The restructuring of the system would be carried out in
conformity with the (amended) Science and Technology Act. This act defines
the main elements for semi-autonomous research institutes; they are:
(i)     a management body with the independence and assured
Government financial support required for the execution of
short-and long-range research programs in response to
present and future needs ensuring continuous improvement
of research management systems;
(ii)    establishment of a research environment in which creative
research can be undertaken through ar& effective management
structure; and



- 10 -
(iii)   ability to attract and retain research personnel of
outstanding calibre, capable of finding solutions to
national development problems.
The Act provides for the transfer of research resources and programs from
relevant ministries to the research institutes. The restructuring of
agricultural research aims to decentralize responsibility for
implementation and increase flexibility to respond to consumer demands.
Several studies have been carried out to prepare the new structure and
these constitute the guidelines for the implementation of the research
strategy. Major components of the action plan to achieve the stated
objectives along the lines of the agreed strategy are outlined below.
Actions
2.08     Organization and management.  All research functions and
activities scattered between the former EAC establishment, SRD and VRD will
be integrated into a single management organization, the new KARI at
Nairobi, which will be governed by a Board of Management with statutory
functions as defined in the amended Science and Technology Act. The
Ministry of Agriculture has been assigned administrative responsibility for
KARI. The Board's Research and Technical Committee and that on Finance and
Administration will oversee the management of the Institute. The day to
day management of KARI will be the responsibility of the Director. The
KARI budget and accounting system would be rationalized so as to provide
up-to-date management information on budgets, expenses and funding per
research center and program.
2.09     Rationalization of Research Centers.  The existing research
centers will be reorganized in national (NRC) and regional (RRC) research
centers. The RRCs will be assigned zonal responsibility on a district and
agroclimatic basis to identify production constraints and devise, test and
apply technological packages suited to these zones. The NRCs will be
concerned with commodity, factor and disciplinary research, covering the
national territory; they will also maintain links with other National and
International research institutes. Senior scientists will have the
responsibility to ensure that activities at the various centers are closely
knit and incorporate external research results. They will be nLtionally
answerable with regard to technical issues pertaining to these programs.
2.10     Research priorities.  A systematic procedure of arriving at
priorities in research activities is being developed. It takes into
account: relative farmgate value of commodities; area cultivated; present
and potential contribution to economic development, food security,
nutrition and employment; market potential; probability of success
including the state of knowledge and the scope for transfer of results in
Kenya and internationally; and research scale, costs and time horizon.
KART will oversee the preparation of long-term strategy proposals for each
RIC and NRC. 'Specialist Committees' will review periodically the long-
term program strategies in close cooperation with the program coordinators
and KARI management (para. 5.12). Responsibility for implementation of
agreed research programs will however, be assigned to the Research Centers.



- 11 -
2.11     Human resources.  KARI will maintain appropriate training programs
to ensure present and future staffing of qualified scientists and introduce
an improved Scheme of Service capable of maintaining a career environment
conducive to ensuring the quality and stability of staff. GOK will see'
resources for the strengthening of the Agricultural University system to
meet the long-term training needs for research. In those scientific and
technical areas where Kenyan expertise is deficient, temporary inputs of
qualified external expert assistance will be sought.
2.12     External financial support.  GOK aims to shape the agricultural
research system in Kenya to meet its future role in a rapidly changing
technological environment. External support to achieve this aim will be
sought. KARI will develop procedures to coordinate external assistance
with a view to support priority programs and avoid research of marginal
utility. It will also coordinate donor supervision and review activities.
III. THE NATIONAL AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH PROJECT
A. Project Obiectives and Strate v
3.01     Major objectives of the proposed Project are to: (i) focus on
priority research programs with a significant likelihood of high and early
returns to investments; (ii) develop technologies that contribute to
increased productivity particularly of small holdings; (iii) intensify
producer participation in research program formulation; and (iv) strengthen
linkages between research and other agricultural services such as extension
as well as between the new agricultural research establishment and other
institutions involved in agricultural research, in particular the
universities, private enterprise and international organizations. In order
to effectively pursue these objectives, GOK, KARI and donors agreed that
NARP would be implemented as an integrated program rather than as isolated
single commodity projects.   Ensuring such implementation of the separately
appraised donor-supported projects which are all part of NARP, would
require strengthened mechanisms for donor coordination (paras. 5.33, 5.34).
3.02     The strategy to achieve these objectives would be for GOK to
reorganize the various agricultural research establishments into a single,
new KARI. This would enable GOK to: (i) establish clear research
priorities and to adhere to these while coordinating external aid; (ii)
develop a long-term strategy, consisting of a KARI corporate plan and
appropriate crop, livestock, factor and disciplinary research programs; and
(iii) develop and introduce improved management systems from headquarters
to field research activities. These improved systems would lead to better
supervision of individual research programs, stations and staff; more
stringent budgeting and financial management; and more meaningful
reporting, monitoring and evaluation (paras. 5.05-5.17).   Research
development requires long-term support in order to be successful and to be
able to respond effectively to consumer demands. Several research
activities, such as breeding programs, often need a timeframe of 10 years



- 12 -
or more to produce results. The strategic planning of research programs is
therefore viewed with a 10-15 year time horizon (pares. 3.10-3.15).
However, planning of research activities over a long period involveq many
uncertainties. The proposed Project is therefore designed as the first 5-
year time slice of a much longer commitment to support the National
Program. The long-term implications with regard to supporting the National
Research Program are understood and accepted by all donors intending to
assist the NARP. An evaluation of progress made during the first phase
would be carried out in year 3 of the Project in order to allow Government,
IDA and other donors to plan the second phase in time to ensure research
continuity (para. 5.17).
B. General Description of the Project
3.03     The proposed NARP 'iould be financed by GOK with external financing
by IDA, and (in parallel) by several other donors (para. 4.04).
Implementation would be phased as a function of available GOK and external
funding, the existing and anticipated implementation capacity of KARI, the
results of the researcher trairting program and access to external expert
assistance. Taking these limiting factors and donor commitments into
account, the overall Project would comprise the following four main
components:
Assistance for the restructuring of KARI as a semi-
autonomous government agency which would undertake research
planning, management, budgeting, supervision, reporting, and
training. USAID and EEC would be major bilateral donors;
the IDA component would support the establishment of
identified elements of the management information systems,
facilitate donor coordination and provide required equipment
and civil works.
The development of KARI's management and research capacity
through staff training and external expert assistance in
selected areas (funded by bilateral donors).
Support for the execution of adaptive research programs
covering all of Kenya's agro-climatic zones and for
equipment and rehabilitation of the Regional Research
Centers. The IDA component would ensure full establishment
of all RRCs, some of which would be aided also by other
donors.
Assistance to implement critical nationally coordinated
commodity, factor and disciplinary research programs for
applied research and to the rehabilitation of the NRCs from
which such programs will be managed. All major donors would
be involved with USAID particularly supporting major cereal
programs, ODA animal health, the Netherlands animal
production and the EEC soil fertility management. The IDA
component would assist approved key programs in terms of
non-salary operating costs, equipment and civil works.



^ 13 -
Important features involved in these components are:
-      Introduction of a new Scheme of Service for KARI staff.
-      A rationalization of the present station network
simultaneausly with the establishment of the RRCs and NRCs
by using all other stations exclusively as testing sites.
-      Improvement of research/extension linkages through joint
activities and better technical training of extension staff.
-      Development of strong linkages of KARI with related research
institutions in Kenya, in other countries and with the
IARCs.
-      Strengthening of KARI's capacity for coordination of donor
assistance to agricultural research.
C. Detailed Features
Establishment of KARI Headquarters
3.04     Institutional development.  Existing legislation (the 1977 Science
and Technology Act, amended in 1979), created several Research Institutes
(such as the Kenya Medical Research Institute which is a model of an
effective public sector research entity conducting valuable medical
research), as well as the restructured KARI; no additional legislation is
required. The restructured KARI was set up in November, 1986 as a semi-
autonomous government agency which consolidated the existing KARI (at
Muguga), the SRD of MOA and the VRD of MOLD, into the reorganized Institute
at Nairobi. The proposed organization and management systems for KARI are
illustrated in Annex II, Chart 1. The proposed IDA-assisted project would
support the establishment of effective management functions, particularly
research planning, programming, budgeting, supervision, monitoring and
evaluation, and donor coordination, which are critical to the success of the
NARP, through providing financing for supplies, equipment, vehicles, and
incremental operating costs on a declining basis. The IDA-supported project
would also finance construction of a headquarters building for KARI. A
proposed Plan of Action (PO') for Project implementation is provided in
Annex III.
3.05     Information systems.  Improved computer technology and computer
repair and maintenance services are now available in Kenya and the
Ministries of Finance, Agriculture and Livestock Development are introducing
computers. The Project therefore makes provisions for the establishment in
KARI of a comprehensive research and management information system (RMIS),
based on a clearly defined organizational structure and revolving around the
use of computers. The system would provide capacity to handle financial,
technical (including library services) and general information (such as word
processing) at the central and research center level and be designed to
enable link-ups with computerized information available in other
institutions such as international research institutes and universities.



- 14 -
An outline plan has been developed to provide appropriate standardized
equipment to KARI headquarters and all National and Regional Research
Centers, simultaneously with the required staff training (Annex VI). The
training programs would include short-term training for senior officers at
recognized institutions in Kenya or abroad and in-service training for other
staff. Adequate trainizIg would specifically be provided for senior computer
officers who would be responsible for introduction, management and
maintenance of the RMIS. The establishment of this system is crucial to:
(i) improve the KARI programming, budgetting and accounting system; and (ii)
generate information on the use of funds per program, research center and
source of funding. The latter will be a prerequisite for channeling donor
funds through the KARI budget as intended by GOK. KARI has already started
the design of an accounting system providing such information and Government
assurances were obtained at negotiations that this would be completed by
March 31, 1988 and that the system would be made operational by July 1,
1988. The proposed Project would support this component through financing
the purchase of equipment, vehicles, software, documentation, staff
training, short-term external expert assistance and recurrent non-salary
operational costs.
Training and External Expert Assistance
3.06     In-service short-term training:  The Project would support short-
term in-service training comprising: (1) short-term courses within Kenya for
both senior and middle level KARI research staff; (ii) short-term courses
at leading institutions abroad including IARCs; (iii) workshops and seminars
in-country and abroad; (iv) training in budgeting, accounting and station
management systems for KARI non-scientific staff- and (v) training programs
for research support staff. The Project would provide assistance to KARI to
meet recurrent and special costs, such as boarding and lodging of
participants, fees to guest faculty, study tours, and costs incurred by
training institutions in conducting the training courses. Short-term
training abroad for senior KARI staff would be primarily supported through
provisions in projects assisted by bilateral donors.
3.07     Degree-related training.  The Project would also provide for post-
graduate degree-related training. It is estimated that KARI would require a
total of about 600 research officers by the fifth year of the Project (Annex
I, Table 2). Most of these would be B.Sc. holders requiring further
training. The Project would train about 60 B.Sc. holders annually for M.Sc
degrees (covering most of the present B.Sc. level research officers of KARI
and about half of newly hired staff); an initial indication of degree
training needs by subject matter area is given in Annex I, Table 2.
Assuming an attrition rate of about 5Z p.a., this training would, over a 5
year period, increase the proportion of M.Sc. holders to about 502 of the
total number of KARI scientists. The Project would also aid KARI in sending
about 12 M.Sc. holders for Ph.D. training annually.  Thus, during the first
few years of the program, close to 1/3 of KARI staff would be on leave
undergoing degree-type training.
3.08     Considering the limited capacity of Nairobi University (Annex I),
only 40Z of M.Sc. degree training (and about 25X of Ph.D. training) would
take place locally; even this would require annual intakes of 24 and 3 for
MSc. and Doctoral programs, respectively. The Faculty of Agriculture of the



- 15 -
University of Nairobi is now managing an intake of about 30 M.Sc. students
annually with difficulty. It would therefore not be possible for the
University to meet the additional demands of KARI, unless support is
provided to the University to: (i) augment its equipment, facilities and
staff; (ii) improve library and information services; (iii) facilitate
fellowships and faculty exchange; (iv) meet recurrent costs; and (v) provide
adequate stipends to post-graduate students. While several donors plan to
provide scholarships to KARI researchers to pursue M.Sc. studies at Nairobi
University, there is no provision for funding and strengthening the
University. However, ISNAR jointly with the University of Nairobi in 1985,
assessed and quantified the requirements for strengthening postgraduate
research training in Agriculture and Veterinary Medicine. The University,
in 1987 developed a draft proposal for strengthening postgraduate and
vocational training programs. These proposals would be further refined for
possible funding under a separate project.
3.09     External expert assistance.  The Project would also provide for
assistance by external experts in key areas. The exact fields of expertise
and durations for each area of research can only be determined after long-
term strategic research proposals have been formulated in all the priority
research programs. However, on the basis of detailed work on four key
programs (paras. 3.10-3.15) and reviews of the approximate needs in other
areas, the second joint-donor mission estimated that in the first 3 to 5
years of the Project, on average more than 30 manyears p.a. would be
required. Provision for such assistance has been made in the areas of
management and organization, monitoring and evaluation, research on maize,
sorghum, soil fertility and soil and water management as well as in several
areas of animal production research. Many other areas such as research in
grain legumes, horticulture, oilseeds, and animal nutrition might remain
without adequate technical support because it would not be realistic to
assume that a sufficient number of trained Kenyan scientists would be
available in these areas in the first few years of the Project. It would be
undesirable to delay the acceleration of research in such critically
important areas until the required Kenyan scientists have been trained. It
would therefore be necessary to identify such commodities and disciplines,
and if possible, arrange for external expert assistance. Such assistance
could be part of "twinning" arrangements to be concluded by KARI with
selected institutions and supported by donors.   All long-term external
expert assistance under the Project would be financed by bilateral donors
under grant terms (mainly USAID, ODA, EEC, the Netherlands and Germany).
Research Program Development
3.10     Adaptive research at RRCs.  One of the major weaknesses of the
current research system has been the lack of adaptive research to ensure
that new recommendations can indeed be translated into messages that are
technically and economically viable for farmers in specific agroclimatic
environments and socio-economic circumstances. To overcome these problems,
the Project would include the strengthening of a network of eight RRCs each
serving an identified agro-ecological zone; in addition there would be two
Centers focussing especially on adaptive livestock research in dry
environments (Map IBRD 20117 and Annex II, Table 1). A long-term strategic
proposal has already been drafted for Katumani's regional program (Annex I),



- 16 -
this would be used as a model by other RRCs. The Katumani RRC's regional
mandate covers the semi-arid zone where moisture is the most limiting
production factor. Human population and animal pressure in this zone are
rapidly increasing and the productive capacity of the resource base is
deteriorating. Priority objectives for research are based on intensive
field studies to diagnose farmers' most critical problems. Some of the main
activities of the proposed program at Katumani would be: (i) the testing of
improved varieties for maize, sorghum and grain legumes as well as the
development of more productive agronomic practices and (inter)cropping
systems; (ii) the identification of high yielding pasture and legume forages
and improved management techniques; (iii) the economic analysis of improved
and existing farming practices; (iv) the development of more productive,
less risky systems of mixed farming; and (v) the dissemination of research
results and in particular the practical training of extension staff (Annex
I). The RRCs would, through on-station and on-farm experiments, develop
packages of agronomic practices and improved management methods for crops
and animals in production systems that would maximize returns to farmers
while attempting to minimize risk. They would also play the key role in
identifying research priorities at the NRCa through feedback (Annex I). In
order to make this possible, research and extension would work much closer
together (paras. 3.18, 5.28). The proposed Project would provide for
rehabilitation of civil works, equipment and research farms, construction of
laboratories and housing, the purchase of new equipment and finance a
(declining) part of the non-salary operating costs of research; it would
also include provisions for training equipment, transport and travel
allowances to facilitate closer research/extension linkages. Effective RRCs
are essential to the viability of the national research system and to
increased agricultural productivity, ensuring adequate support to all RRCs
would therefore be a priority objective of the IDA-assisted project.
3.11     National commodity, factor anddisciplinary research.  The sixteen
NRCs with their tentatively assigned research programs are given in Annex
II, Table 2 (see also map IBRD 20117). Initially, the nationally
coordinated research programs would emphasize adaptive research on critical
aspects of important food grains, livestock products and production factors
(for the relative priorities and phasing of these programs see paras. 5.08 -
5.10 and Annex III). The NRCs would have principal responsibility for
generating and testing improved technologies for commodities, factors of
production or disciplines. Each would build up a multidisciplinary team of
scientists in relation to the production problems to be addressed. KARI
would also use donor assistance for the establishment of a Research Fund in
order to contract special projects to other Kenyan institutions and/or
individual scientists best qualified in a particular area of research need.
For basic research information, the NRCs would draw on other institutions in
Kenya (e.g. Nairobi University) and outside (for example the IARCs). All
the NRCs given in Annex II, Table 2, except the Ahero National Irrigation
Research Center, presently exist. They would be rehabilitated, involving
civil works, research farm development, purchase of farm and laboratory
equipment as well as transport and would be provided with increased non-
salary operating funds and essential facilities to carry out priority
research.



- 17 -
3.12     The formulation of long-term proposals for applied research is in
an advanced stage for maize, tickborne diseases and soil and water
management; these are of high priority and would be initiated during the
first two Project years. These programs were appraised during the two
joint-donor missions; they are discussed in summary below and in more detail
in Annex I. Estimated training requirements for each program have been
summarized in Annex I, Table 2. Important aspects of proposed programs for
wheat, grain legumes and some animal production and health programs are also
given in Annex I because these research subjects are to be initiated at an
early date (Annex III). Long-term strategic proposals and detailed annual
programs for all nationally coordinated research areas would be prepared by
KARI during Project implementation according to the time schedule given in
the POA (Annex III).
3.13     GOK's Sessional Paper No. 1 of 1986 assigns highest priority to the
maize research program. Maize is the major staple of Kenya and among food
crops, occupies by far the largest area (Annex I, Table 3). Past research
on maize resulted in the development of several superior hybrids and
varieties as well as the generation of important information on improved
agronomic practices in particular for the high potential areas and for sole
crop systems. Much less is known for the more marginal areas where maize is
often grown in mixed cropping systems. The revitalized maize research
program would be based on a careful assessment of the needs of the different
agroclimatic zones (Annex I). It would be coordinated from the Kitale NRC
with cooperative programs at the Kakamega, Katumani, Embu and Mtwapa
Research Centers. Close cooperative ties with the relevant IARCs would be
developed and some external expert assistance (in particular in plant
breeding and agronomy) would be needed in the initial years. The national
maize research program would involve breeding, agronomy, plant protection
and production systems; it would give special attention to higher
productivity, pest and disease resistance and more efficient use of high
cost inputs and of labor. Maize is grown by nearly every Kenyan farmer and
strong linkages with the network of RRCs would therefore be essential.
3.14     Animal health research on tickborne diseases is also of high
priority. Of the four tickborne diseases, East Coast Fever has the greatest
economic impact. About 24Z of agricultural production Et farmgate and about
60% of Kenya's farm families are affected through dipping costs and/or death
losses caused by tickborne diseases. Dipping programs to eradicate the
vector are threatened by tolerance to acaricide. The proposed research
program at the National Veterinary Research Center, Muguga would generate
technology for a new diagnostic test against Heartwater, and better sero-
diagnostic tests for Anaplasmosis, Babesiosis and East Coast Fever; it would
also aim at the development of a quadrivalent vaccine against tickborne
diseases which would reduce the need for dipping and result in significant
savings in terms of animal values. Close ties would be developed with
relevant programs at the International Laboratory for Research on Animal
Diseases and the International Center for Insect Physiology and Ecology
(both these IARCs are based in Kenya). The research results would be of
special importance to small-scale dairy farmers. Existing research
facilities and research staff at Muguga are of good quality; some
specialized external assistance would be required. The research program



- 18 -
would comprise subprojects on all four tickborne diseases with each sub-
project having determined a specific set of time-bound activities to achieve
its objectives (for details see Annex I).
3.15     Factor research on soil and water management has been neglected in
the past and most available solutions are difficult to implemernt and
maintain on smallholdings. Although soil and water management has now been
given high priority by Government, the available manpower and facilities
limit the initial size of the proposed research effort. The envisaged
program on soil and water management would consist of two main components,
one located at the Muguga NRC and the other at the Katumani NRC (possibly
followed later by another sub-program in a more arid environment). Research
would be coordinated from Katumani; at the national level this would involve
a survey of soil degradation and erosion hazard in Kenya, establishment of a
vegetasive cover data bank and assistance in technical writing and training.
The sub-project for semi-arid areas would continue studies on the
relationships of runoff and erosion to soil and rainfall characteristics
while that for high-potential areas would focus on development of more
appropriate soil conservation structures. Both sub-projects would aim at
quantifying the impact of erosion on productivity. However, the basic goal
of the new program would be the generation of more productive farming
systems that conserve the resource base; this would be achieved through
close cooperation with several RRCs (Annex I).
Other Project Features
3.16     Scheme of Service.  The Project would support the introduction of a
revised Scheme of Service which would result in better scales of pay,
service conditions and incentives for research staff (para. 5.20). While
the details of the Scheme are yet to be finalized, an outline was reviewed
during appraisal. Under the Project, staff salaries would be entirely
financed by GOK.
3.17     Station rationalization.  The reorganization of agricultural
research would also involve a streamlining of the network of research
stations on the basis of their specific mandates (Annex II, Tables 1 and 2).
The lead responsibilities of all Research Centers would be reviewed but
especially in case of Kakamega (sorghum), Mwea Tebere (cotton), Kitale
(pastures and fodder), Muguga (dairy, plant protection and animal health),
and Naivasha (cattle production). Required reallocations would be made
before July 1, 1988 and followed by phasing out some current activities at
affected stations (para. 5.23). Those research stations not assigned a
National or Regional function would be maintained as testing sites to
provide adequate coverage for Kenya's widely diverse agroclimatic
conditions.
3.18     Research/extension linkages.  Research would start with the
farmers' problems and opportunities and conclude with solutions and
technologies that enable farmers to increase their incomes. In order for
research to achieve this end-objective, it is essential that 4t forges
effective linkages with extension as well as with other related specialised
research institutions and services (para. 5.28, 5.29). For the National and



- 19 -
Regional Centers, these linkages have been schematically illustrated in
Annex I, Chart 1. KARI would design and implement specific mechanisms that
would ensure a two way flou of information between farmers, extension and
research, and provide for a joint diagnosis of existing production
constraints, both biological and socio-economic.
3.19     Linkages with other research centers.  No national research system
can stand by itself. KARI would therefore establish linkages with other
Kenyan Centers (Nairobi University, Research Foundations etc.) and IARCs as
well as with national research centers in other countries engaged in similar
types o! research (para. 5.31). The costs of such cooperation would be
supported by the Project.
3.20     Donor coordination.  Several donors are currently providing
assistance to ongoing research projects; they would in the future be
involved in support to compc ants and aspects of the NARP that have been
rated as being of high prior. y for Kenya's future agricultural development
laras. 5.08 - 5.10 and Annex III). Donor coordination would therefore be
essential with regard to: (i) reaching agreements to phase out existing
projects which are not consistent with the NARP; (ii) the selection of new
activities and the redirection of donor assistance to higher NARP priority
areas; (iii) disbursement procedures; and (iv) monitoring, evaluation and
supervision. KARI would, by March 31, 1988 set-up a special unit in the
Planning and Manpower Development Division to facilitate donor coordination
and the collection, analysis and distribution of information required by
donors (paras. 5.33, 5.34). The cost of donor coordination-related
activities would be included in the KARI Headquarters operational costs and
supported by the Project.
IV. PROJECT COSTS AND FINANCING
A. Proiect Costs
4.01     The total costs for the five-year time slice (1987/88-91/92) of the
NARP are estimated at about KL 183.7 M (nearly US$ 204 M), including KL38.2
M (US$22.0 M) for physi^al and price contingencies and KL4.5 M (US$ 5 M) for
taxes and duties. Foreign exchange costs are estimated at about US$ 61.8 M,
or 30Z of total Project costs. Table 4.1 summarizes the Project costs
according to the three main components (for details see Annex IV, Tables 1
through 5). The ertimated costs are based on the GOK's Project Preparation
Report for the NARP with further refinements made during the IDA appraisal
particularly with regard to the detailed "model" research programs (paras.
3.10-3.15). Additional updating will be required from time to time given
the program nature of the Project and the need to firm-up during Project
implementation, the detailed costs according to agreed long-term proposals
for research programs as well as the financing plan (para. 4.04). While
Table 4.1 indicates that a high proportion of the funds would be allocated
to the sixteen NRCs and related commodity, factor and disciplinary programs,
the structure and content of the research system would ensure that a
substantial proportion of these costs would be used to directly support the
RRCs and their network of adaptive research activities.

4.02     An indicative allocation of funds according to the Project's five
main categories of research is: crops (54Z); soil and water management (62);
animal production (13X); animal health (192); and range and arid lands (82)
(see Annex IV, Tables 6 and 7). The precise amount of funds that eventually
would be allocated to the Research Centers, including the RRC network and to
the specific research programs would be worked out in detail and adjusted as
appropriate during Project implementation at the time of preparation of the
Annual Work Programs and Budgets (para. 5.33). It is expected that KARI's
new management and budgeting systems will ensure that the funds allocated,
reflect sound priorities and balance in terms of Research Centers and
programs.
4.03     Project costs for various expenditure items including construction,
vehicles and equipment, are estimated on the basis of prices prevailing in
mid-1986, but adjusted to mid-1987 constant prices. The taxes and duties are
relatively small since it is expected that GOK would waive these costs on
imported vehicles and equipment procured under the Project, including a
waiver already provided for items funded by bilateral donors. Physical
contingencies vary from 0-152, with an overall average of 52. This small
percentage reflects Government's intention to contain the number of
personnel added to the research establishment (para. 5.20). Price
contingencies are in accordance with Bank estimates of expected inflation
rates for local and foreign costs. The rates of price escalation in Kenya
pounds/shillings and in US dollars are expected to be offset by a
countervailing gradual depreciation of the Kenya shilling. Salaries and
related allowances reflect the proposed improved Scheme of Service currently



- 21 -
being worked out by GOK. The actual Project costs and funding during
implementation would be adjusted according to the final approved salary
structure for KARI staff, the level and mix of financing provided by donors
and GOK's budgetary support (para. 4.07).
B. Financing
4.04     The financing plan including contingencies, according to
expenditure category, is summarized in Table 4.2. This financing plan is
tentative, since the GOK forward budget (1987/88 - 1989/90) recently
approved by Treasury, includes only the donor funds formally agreed with GOK
as of end-1986 (i.e. USAID) and excludes funds from other donors which are
in the process of being formally committed. Also, the fitting and phasing
of Project requirements with donor interests still has to be worked out in
detail and formally agreed. It is expected that the final 1987/88 budget
estimates currently under preparation and GOK's rolling forward budget to be
updated by end-1987, will reflect the other donor funds which are intended
to be committed by end 1987.

4.05     The five main donors that plan to support NARP during the five
year period are IDA, USAID, EEC, ODA and the Netherlands. These donors
together would provide about US$ 87.5 million equivalent, or about 43X of
total Project costs (Annex IV, Table 3, 4 and 8). Most of these donors are
at various stages of report preparation and approval by their respective
managements or Governments; USAID funds are already agreed and being used
by KARI. Formal agreements between other donors and GOK are scheduled to
be finalized prior to IDA Credit effectiveness. In addition, there are six
other donors (Annex IV, Table 8) who intend to support the Project for a



- 22 -
total of about US$ 10 million equivalent. These other donors include
Australia, Canada, West Germany, Japan, Sweden and UNDP/FAO. To the extent
these other donor funds exceed those assumed in Table 4.2, this would
reduce GOK's financial contribution. At negotiations, assurances were
obtained from GOK that any modifications to the proposed financing plan
would be discussed and agreed between IDA, GOK and other donors as part of
the periodic discussions on the Project's POA (para. 5.33 and Anmex III).
In order to facilitate the use of donor funds, virtually all funding would
be provided on a parallel financing basis. Some expenses (e.g. incremental
non-salary operating expenses), would be funded on a joint basis in order
to facilitate the disbursement and accounting of donor funds for such
items.
4.06     Expenditure items to be financed with the IDA Credit would
include: (i) incremental non-salary operating costs on a declining basis;
(ii) vehicles; (iii) equipment; (iv) civil works; (v) in-service training
and degree-related courses (seminars, study tours, post-graduate training);
and (vi) consultancy services as part of the use of the PPF funds.
Contracts for use of consultants are estimated to cost less than US$100,000
equivalent for each collective assignment. IDA funding includes a
Government-requested Project Preparation Facility (PPF) of US$700,000 which
IDA formally approved. The PPF would help finance detailed preparation and
Project start-up activities. Although use of IDA funds has been
identified, there would be flexibility to cover priority financing gaps as
they arise as long as IDA approval would have been obtained for the
program(s) concerned.
4.07     All funds for the Project (including IDA financing) would be
reflected in GOK budgets and would be channelled from Treasury through MOA
as grants to KARI in accordance with existing GOK procedures. Table 4.2
indicates that the counterpart funds to be provided by GOK (US$105.7
million or 522 of the total) would cover primarily salaries and allowances
and some non-operating expenses not funded by donors. GOK intends to: (i)
reflect agreed donor funding in its budgetary allocations; (ii) provide
adequate budget allocations to fund the NARP; (iii) decrease gradually the
current ratio of salary costs to total recurrent costs; and (iv) adjust
research programs on the basis of the agreed priorities and phasing (paras.
5.08- 5.10 and Annex III), in the event of shortfalls in financing.
C. Procurement
4.08     Procurement arrangements are summarized in Table 4.3; figures in
parenthesis are the respective amounts financed by IDA. All figures
include both physical and price contingencies. Since most of the donor
funds would be provided on a parallel financing basis, the procurement
methods used by KARI would be in accordance with the respective procedures
agreed with each donor concerned. For similar expenditure items (e.g.
computers and office, laboratory and farm equipment), financed by more than
one donor, KARI would closely coordinate procurement activities to ensure
adequate standardization and effective use of donor funds.
4.09     About 211 of IDA's Credit would be procured through International
Competitive Bidding, about 53? through Local Competitive Bidding, 192



- 23 -
through other procurement methods and 7Z without prescribable procurement
methods (including various non-salary operating expenses). Procurement for
66Z of the total Project costs falls under N/A, or where no prescribable
procurement method will be applicable. These are expenditures for salaries
and staff costs, training and operating costs which would be incurred in
accordance with KARI's financial and other operational regulations which
are acceptable to IDA. Project funds would be disaggregated and channelled
to KARI Headquarters and to various Research Centers. Bidding packages
would be prepared on an annual basis consistent with annual budgetary
provisions, stipulating packages for items that would be procured by KARI
Headquarters and by individual Research Centers. ICB thresholds would be
US$1,000,000 for civil works and US$50,000 for plant and equipment;
preference for local manufacturers would be allowed. Contracts involving
expenditures in excess of US$250,000 and 50,000 equivalent for civil works
and plant/equipment respectively, would be submitted to IDA for prior
review. At negotiations, assurances were obtained from GOK that KARI would
be authorized to procure civil works, materials and services in accordance
with approved procedures for state corporations. An outline of KARI's
procurement procedures was reviewed during IDA appraisal and found to be in
conformity with existing GOK procedures. During negotiations, assurances
were obtained from GOK that KARI's final procurement procedures would be
acceptable to IDA.
