D. Disbursements
4.10     Disbursement of IDA credit funds would be made for non-salary
expenditure categories which are connected with KARI Headquarters
activities or long-term research proposals reviewed and approved by IDA.
Such approval would be a condition of disbursement for such research
rroposals. This procedure would include the involvement of RMESA staff in
processing and submission of disbursement applications; these staff would
also assist in procurement and closely supervise adherence to the agreed
POA (Annex III). Disbursements would be against full documentation for
capital expenditures exceeding US$50,000 and against statements of
expenditure for all other items. The percentages to be financed by IDA for
the different categories of expenditure are shown in Annex IV, Table 9. A
Project Special Account is not included at this time because GOK and the
Bank Group are still in the process of working out the final arrangements
for such accounts.
4.11     The NARP is a five year timeslice of an ongoing long-term program
in agricultural research. Given the nature of NARP, the available standard
disbursement profiles are not applicable. All past projects in Kenya which
provide the basis of the standard profile involved essentially new
activities, and in most cases the creation of new institutions. KARI's
activities have already begun with support of USAID and start-up will be
further accelerated by the PPF which has been approved by IDA. KARI's
independent procurement authority would facilitate improved procurement and
disbursement. A significant part of IDA financing relates to the recurrent
non-salary operational costs of KARI and the Research Centers; purchase of
equipment and construction of civil works is mostly scheduled during the
first four Project years and would therefore be easily completed by the end
of the first phase. The disbursement period for the Project would, for
these reasons, be estimated at six years (for Disbursement Schedule, see
Annex V, Table 10). It is anticipated that the final disbursements of the
IDA Credit would be made by June 30, 1993, about 12 months after completion
of the Project's implementation and closing date of June 30, 1992.
E. Accounts and Audits
4.12     As a new institution, KARI would develop its own specific
accounting system. At appriasal, it was agreed that during its initial
year (i.e. 1987/88), KARI would operate on the GOK accounting system which
is already in use irn KARI's constituent parts. This system provides for
budgeting and accounting by spending research station, item (type of
expenses) and head (major program classification); KARI's future financial
staff are familiar with this system. Although cumbersome, this system is
an adequate means to record expenditures and is acceptable to IDA during
KARI's initial period of operation. A specific accounting system, however,
will be required to account for donor contributions as this does not form
part of the regular GOK accounting system. This system would:
(i)    identify expenditures for reimbursement by donors and
generate the disbursement claims;
(ii)   account for direct donor payments and contributions in
kind; and



- 25 -
(iii) produce statements of amounts due (or received) from
donors and the application of these amounts(by station,
item and head).
A specific system would also account for assets and liabilities, as the
Government accounting system does not account for these systematically. An
inventory and evaluation of assets would be carried out and an opening
balance sheet established with assets valued as of July 1, 1988. It was
agreed at negotiations that this opening balance sheet would be submitted
to IDA by July 1, 1988.
4.13     During 1987/88, KARI intends to develop a more responsive
accounting system. This would provide improved and integrated accounting
and management information that would meet concurrently the requirements of
GOK, KARI managers and donors. The main features would be: (i)
computerized accounting, providing rapid data treatment and up-to-date
reports (para. 3.05); and (ii) provision of analysis of costs by research
program enabling KARI and donors to account for expenditures according to
type, Research Center and program. This accounting and analysis would also
permit a comparative analysis of costs of each program, and hence assist
program evaluation.
4.14     It was agreed that consultants would be engaged by KARI before
September 30, 1987 to help in the development and introduction of the new
system, including its application on the proposed microcomputers. The
consultants would also help with training of staff in operating the new
systems. Terms of reference for this assistance have been agreed (Annex
VI). It is expected that this consultancy would begin once the Finance
Manager and his staff are in place, early in fiscal year 1987/88.
4.15     KARI would produce a balance sheet and income and expenditure
statement annually, beginning with the fiscal year 1987/88. In addition,
the accounts would show in detail the source and applications of all funds
managed by KARI and would present a statement of IDA funds showing source
by credit category and application by project component. KARI accounts
will be audited by the Auditor General (Corporations) or a professional
firm nominated by him which is acceptable to IDA (Annex II). The work of
the Auditor General (Corporations) will be supplemented by an Internal
Audit Division within KARI reporting through the Director to the Board of
Management. KARI inherits all the accounting staff of its constituent
units; these are adequate to operate the initial accounting system. At
KARI headquarters, a central accounting unit will be established during the
first year, under a Finance Manager. It was agreed at negotiations that
the audited eccounts including -he audit certificate for the statements of
expenditure, would be submitted to IDA within nine months after the end of
the fiscal year, beginning in 1987/88.



- 26 -
V. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Overall Organization and Management
KARI Tasks
5.01     A schematic overview of the new agricultural research structure is
given in Annex II, Chart 1. The Kenya Agricultural Research Institute has
responsibility for all crop, livestock, factor and disciplinary research
funded under the GOK budget or by external funding obtained by KARI.
Agricultural research is also carried out by Nairobi University, Research
Foundations and private companies; KARI would closely cooperate with these
agencies through reciprocal participation in Research Center Committees and
cooperation at the level of the RRCs (para. 5.26). KARI would, within the
national strategy, further evolve its research policy based on national
development goals and priorities through liaison with the Ministries of:
Agriculture; Livestock Development; Finance; and Planning, all of which
have representatives on KARI's Board. An effective working relationship
would be developed with the newly established (June, 1987) Ministry of
Research, Science and Technology (MRST). KARI would implement the NARP and
carry out research activities through Nationally Coordinated Research
Programs and through RRCs responsible for adaptive research. It would
monitor the progress and quality of research output through effective
management information and control systems.
5.02     The KARI organigram (Annex II, Chart 1), provides for a Board of
Management, a Managing Director, and three Departments, each headed by a
Deputy Director (Crops, Soils and Water; Livestock; and Finance Planning
and Administration). The Board of Management was nominated by the Minister
of Agriculture in December, 1986 and approved by Kenya's President; the
Board has since met periodically, approved NARP and appointed KARI's
Director and two of the Deputy Directors. The members of the Board include
representatives of Nairobi University and private enterprise. The Board
deliberates on policy issues and approves the KARI budgets and Annual
Reports; it has approved the first year's (87/88) research program and
budget proposed in NARP. This work plan includes further rationalization
of the National Research Program and the detailed preparation of additional
commodity, factor and disciplinary research programs (POA, Annex III). A
large measure of authority is left to KARI management and day to day
management would be fully delegated to the Director. This organization is
consistent with KARI's status and with the requirements of the (amended)
Science and Technology Act.
Status of Prtparation
5.03     Project preparation was briefly discussed in para. 2.04.
Preparatory activities accelerated after NARP had been accepted as a
framework for reorganization in October, 1986. Further detailed work was
done on proposals for several nationally coordinated and regional research
programs (para. 3.10-3.15 and Annex I). The establishment of KARI began in
November, 1986 with the secondment of several senior research staff, the
setting up of temporary physical premises, provision of a special budget
for the remainder of fiscal year 1986/87 and of a forward budget through



- 27 -
1989/90. A USAID-supported project to improve KARI's organization and
management structure and to strengthen research in maize, sorghum and
millets became effective December 20, 1986. As part of the IDA appraisal,
consultants prepared working papers on the restructuring of research on
animal production and health, the establishment of KARI's information
system and the Project's civil works (Annex VI). With funding from the
approved PPF, KARI would:
(1)     edit and publish the NARP proposal;
(ii)    finalize earlier prepared long-term research proposals;
(iii)   start detailed preparation in those other areas where
strengthening is scheduled to be initiated during the
first two Project years;
(iv)    complete the preparation of administrative, financial and
budgetary procedures to be followed by the new KARI;
(v)     update plans for training and external expert assistance
and organize training in research management, information
systems, research farm operation, etc.
5.04     With senior KARI staff in position (para. 5.02), it was agreed at
negotiations that the appointment of other key staff, especially the Deputy
Director Finance, Planning and Administration, the Assistant Directors of
the different KARI research divisions (Annex II, Chart 1), the Center
Directors and Research Program Coordinators, including the head of the
Manpower Development and Training section, would be given first priority;
appointment of these staff is a condition of Project effectiveness. It was
in addition agreed during negotiations that the Assistant Directors,
Planning and Manpower Development, Finance and Administration and also the
Section Head Planning, Programming and Budgeting would be appointed by July
1, 1988.   During negotiations, Government also agreed that the
implementation schedule of the POA (Annex III) would be followed and
eventual later amendments made following review with IDA and other donors
every year no later than January 31, beginning in 1988. Draft job
descriptions for key KARI staff are provided in Annex II, Appendix 1; these
would be approved by the Board before December 31, 1987. Most KARI staff
would be provided by transfer of part of the staff from the existing
agricultural research organizations before July 1, 1988. This would be
done by decision of the Minister of Agriculture after the approval of
KARI's Board of Management.
Supervision Responsibilities
5.05     Supervisory roles are explicit in the organization proposed for
KARI (Annex II, Chart 1). The Director would supervise the Deputy
Directors. The Deputy Directors would supervise the Assistant Directors.
The Assistant Directors would supervise the Directors of Research Centers.
This would require frequent visits to the Centers by all management staff
to evaluate work and to give technical and administrative guidance. The
Planning, Programming and Budgeting Section of the Department of Finance,
Planning and Administration would carry responsibility for donor



- 28 -
coordination (paras. 5.33, 5.34). This Department's Monitoring and
Evaluation Section would provide KARI management with information and
analysis needed to carry out their supervisory activities. The Annual
Reports and other written work produced by the Research Centers, would be a
major source of information used by KARI management in supervising the
stations. Each Center Director would be fully responsible for all aspects
of the Center's program including the sanctioning of expenditures and
decisions on staff promotions; he would therefore need to supervise the
relevance and quality of research at the Center, the performance of staff
undertaking the research, as well as the preparation of annual work
programs and reports. The national commodity, factor and disciplinary
program coordinators would have a technical supervision function for the
research programs assigned to them. Supervisory techniques and procedures,
as well as reporting formats are now being developed by KARI's acting
managers and would be ready for use immediately after KARI becomes fully
staffed.
Information and Reporting Systems
5.06     KARI's section on Manpower Development and Planning would be
responsible for the establishment of th 'MIS (para. 3.05) including
detailed planning, training and proper muintenance and updating of computer
hard and software. Under the new KARI, the completion of seasonal or
annual reports on progress of the research program by each researcher and
team leader would be a condition for approval of the next year's program.
A common criticism of the research system has been the failure of
scientists to make the importance and implications of scientific,
technological, or other advances known to non-scientists in simple non-
technical language. KARI would therefore devise appropriate reporting
styles for use in relations with Government leaders, planners and decision
makers on the one hand, and for extension personnel and farmers on the
other. This would help create the desired public awareness of the results
of agricuitural research. A Publications Unit to be established at KARI
headquarters would provide special assistance in the preparation of such
publications.
5.07     It was agreed at negotiations that KARI would design reporting
formats and guidelines by March 31, 1988 and introduce these by July 1,
1988. The timely preparation and submission of annual reports by Research
Centers and researchers on each of their assigned programs and projects
would be monitored by their immediate supervisors: program leaders,
coordinators and Center Directors (Annex II). Compilation of the Research
Center reports would be the responsibility of the Center Directors; these
reports would be approved by the respective Assistant Directors and Deputy
Directors. KARI would each year in May, organize an Annual Research
Conference to deliberate on research themes and/or issues of national
importance. KARI's Annual Report would be based on the station reports and
approved by the Board (para. 5.02). Essential to an efficient research
system is also the production of high ruality publications to share new
information with coworkers in other national (and international)
institutions. KARI would therefore by July 1, 1988 set up a screening,
review and editing committee to ensure reliability and quality of material
offered for publication by KARI scientists to scientific journals or
published on a more informal basis.



- 29 -
Research Priority Setting and Phasing
5.08     The Government has recently (Sessional Paper No. 1 of 1986),
assigned high priority to agricultural research on seven commodities that
are of critical importance to Kenya's future: maize, wheat, milk, meat,
coffee, tea and horticulture. Research on coffee and tea is financed by
Commodity Boards encompassing all producers. Research in these areas is
well-established (Annex I) and it is therefore not funded under NARP. A
first attempt to arrive at a more precise statement of priorities including
research on factors of production (e.g. soils and water) and disciplines
(e.g. plant protection) and to clearly assign responsibility for major
areas of research was made in the 1985 ISNAR report (para. 2.04). After
minor adjustments, these were accepted by GOK in the preparation report of
NARP; potential donors, in October 1986, also expressed broad support for
the basic thrust of these proposals. The three identified priority groups
are (Annex I, Table 3):
(i)     maize, wheat, sorghum and millet, grain legumes, fruits
and vegetables, root and tuber crops, seed technology,
dairy production, beef production, coffee, tea, and soil
and water management;
(ii)    rice, oilseeds, cotton, sugarcane, treecrops, pyrethrum,
range and pasture, sheep and goats, food technology and
agricultural engineering;
(iii)   other small grains, other fiber crops, agroforestry,1
flower culture, poultry, pigs, camels, and germplasm
collection and conservation.
5.09     The actual timing of the full implementation of the rehabilitation
of a research program would be phased on the basis of: (i) its priority
status; (ii) the lead time necessary to prepare comprehensive and
satisfactory strategies and a proposed long-term research program; (iii)
KARI's capacity to guide preparation, review proposals and approve these;
and (iv) the limitations of available budgetary and manpower resources.
The existing centers would however, as of July 1, 1987 receive adequate
non-salary operating funds that will permit the effective use of staff
assigned on the basis of the rationalisation of the research station
network and mandates (para. 3.17, 5.23).
5.10     An indicative sequence of the full-scale strengthening of key
research programs which incorporates GOK-stated priorities, is given in the
POA (Annex III).  The proposed sequence and timing is also based on the
experience gained in Kenya during the preparation period and on national
concerns in terms of research priorities (para. 2.10).  The phasing plan
would be approved by the KARI Board's Scientific and Technical Committee
before July 1, 1988, The phasing plan would be regularly reviewed and
further refined in consultations between GOK and donors (para. 5.33). If
1/ Agroforestry (and forestry) research is a primary responsibility of the

being strengthened under the Agroforestry Research Network for Africa

overall Project implementation would be faster than expected, funds would1

of non-salary operating funds for ongoing programs (para. 4.07), the
strengthening of additional research programs would be delayed beyond what
is now envisaged in the POA.
Research Planning, Programming and Budgeting
5.11     The Research Committee Structure.  Commodity, factor and
disciplinary research programs would be coordinated nationally. Typically,
a coordinated research program would consist of a coordinating center
located at an NRC and a number of cooperating centers located at other NRCs
or in the different RRCs, depending on the importance of the commodity or
the product in the different regions. These programs with the
corresponding Research Centers from which they are organized, are given in
Annex II, Table 2. Each nationally coordinated program will be headed by a
coordinator, who would be a senior scientist capable of providing
scientific leadership (job description, Annex II, Appendix 1). The
coordinator would be in charge of a multi-disciplinary team of KARI
researchers, located at the coordinating NRC and cooperating centers. KARI
would establish for each of the nationally coordinated research programs, a
Program or Factor Committee. This Committee would be headed by the program
coordinator; it would be responsible for the regular annual evaluation of
the past year's research results and the design of the next year's
experimental program; it would meet at least once a year. It would also
ensure linkages with related research programs and the continued relevance
of the research program to farmers' problems. The different Program and
Factor Committees would therefore be composed of all participating
researchers, (including those involved in adaptive research at RRCs),
representatives of closely related programs (e.g. at Universities or at
privately funded centers) and of MOA and MOLD, in particular of senior
subject mastter specialists of the extension services. Assurances were
obtained at negotiations that KARI would establish the Program and Factor
Committees by December 31, 1987.
5.12     A system of Specialist Committees for each major commodity, factor
and disciplinary program similar to that which functioned successfully
under the EAC would be re-established to guide KARI research and to link
research with related institutions. Each broad-based Specialist Committee
would be led by the respective KARI Deputy Director. Members would be
drawn from institutions with relevant ongoing work and those that make use
of research results (including the University, the private sector, the
IARCs, KARI as well as MOA, MOLD and especially the extension service).
The respective Deputy Director would be responsible for periodically (say
every 3 years) convening Specialist Committee meetings, and (in conjunction
with the coordinator) prepare reports and act as secretary to the
Committee. These Specialist Committees would be called initially to review
and approve long-term research programs and strategies for the particular
commodity, factor or discipline; these would provide the basis for detailed
annual research programming. At the time of initial research program
formulation and later when periodic in-depth reviews would be conducted to
consider results and review the need for reorientation, experts



- 31 -
representing donors supporting the particular program, would be invited to
participate. Government agreement was obtained at negotiations that
Specialist Committees for the major research programs be established and
called for advice before final KARI approval of such programs.
5.13     In addition, Committees would be established at each Research
Center for the purpose of designing long-term strategies, reviewing ongoing
Center programs and to develop new annual research programs at the
beginning of each year (Annex II). These Research Center Committees would
maintain close contact with the KARI Commodity and Factor Committees, such
that Center programs would reflect the aggregate of relevant national
commodity and/or disciplinary/factor research programs as well as the
specific needs of the region in which each Center is located. Center
Committees would therefore include district development and extension staff
from MOA and MOLD, farmers and representatives of local parastatal and
private enterprises working in agriculture or agro-industry in order to
facilitate a diverse input into research programming. At negotiations
Government agreement was obtained that all Research Center Committees would
be established not later than December 31, 1987.
5.14     The Programming and Budgeting Cycle.  The annual research
budgeting exercise would follow the schedule outlined below:
June 30:         The Directors of RRCs and NRCs, as well as commodity,
factor and disciplinary program coordinators would submit
their annual reports for the past year and work plans for
the coming year iacluding drafts for two more years, to
KARI's Planning, Programming and Budgeting Section.
Research Center Committees would meet well before this date
(para. 5.25). Meetings of KARI's national Commodity and
Factor Committees (and in some cases the Specialist
Committees) as well as KARI's Annual Agricultural Research
Conference (para. 5.07) would also take place during the
preceding months.
July 15:         (i) Treasury budget guidelines would be communicated to MOA
and MOLD which in turn would provide budget guidelines for
KARI, as part of the forward budget exercise; (ii) KARI
would receive the previous year's financial report, KARI
also would receive reports from any Specialist Committees
which would have met by this time (para. 5.12); (iii)
KARI's Planning, Programming and Budgeting Section, after
discussion with the Development Planning Divisions of MOA
and MOLD, would prepare a first draft annual national
research program (funding for which would begin July 1 of
the next year) by commodity, factor, discipline and
station; and (iv) the program would be costed.
July 31:         The Planning, Programming and Budgeting Section of KARI
would submit its program and budget to KARI management
which would approve or modify the plan.



- 32 -
August 1S       The approved program would be submitted to the NRCs and
RRCs for refinement and the preparation of detailed
research programs. Whenever tniere would be new programs,
old programs dropped or modifications to existing programs,
these would be elaborated into work plans for each
researcher.
September 15:   The detailed research programs and budgets by commodity and
station would be resubmitted to KARI management for
approval and subsequently to KARI's Board.
January 1:       The annual budget exercise would make final budget
allocations by research station and program, based on the
work programs developed in the above process, for the
fiscal year beginning July 1 of the same year. However, it
would permit modification of the forward budget and the
research plan. The results would be approved by KARI's
Board.
In effect there would be two planning exercises for each year: the 3 year
moving forward research plan and budget, and the annual plan and budget.
The KARI Managing Director with the Deputy Director in charge of Finance,
Planning and Administration would be responsible for the timely execution
of these activities.
Monitoring, Evaluation and Review
5.15     Under the NARP, a monitoring and evaluation (M & E) system would
be established to ensure that research is continuously steered in the
proper direction, that the scarce resources available are allocated
according to pre-determined priorities and that they are effectively
utilized. The M & E Section of KARI's Planning and Manpower Development
Division would undertake this function (Annex II, Chart 1). The monitoring
system would review both financial and physical progress. Evaluation would
look at questions of efficiency, effectiveness and impact of research
programs; it would also help in future planning. The data used for M & E
would be provided in standard formats (designed by the M & E Section) by
the officers directly responsible for a given task. M & E systems would be
established for:
(i)     KARI's entire program (hence to be established at KARI
headquarters);
(ii)    each nationally coordinated research program;
(iii)   each Research Center; and
(iv)    the individual research projects.
Feedback would be provided to the responsible officers, immediate
supervisors and KARI management. The M & E Section at KARI headquarters
would also develop and implement a comprehensive evaluation system of the
long-term effect of research projects. In order to do this, it would be
necessary to compile relevant data on agricultural commodities in different



- 33 -
production zones including estimated real and potential yvlIds, benefits
from research, and actual production costs. KARI's M & E Section would
make use of the results of the Annual Research Conference (para. 5.07). A
more detailed description of the proposed M & E systems is given in Annex
II. Agreement was obtained at negotiations that KARI would prepare for IDA
review and comment, a detailed work plan for M & E and that these H & E
systems would be implemented by July 1, 1988.
5.16     The Research Center is the place at which it is most important to
establish an effective monitoring system. Each Center Director with the
assistance of research program coordinators, would be responsible for the
quality and the timely implementation of the Center's programs; he must be
constantly aware of the indicators of effectiveness including expenditure
control, timing of experimental work, staff requirements, maintenance of
the Center, preparation of reports, liaison with headquarters and linkages
with the local community. Additionally, there would be quarterly meetings
of program leaders and coordinators at each Center to ensure regular
progress review, adequate consultation among senior staff and the removal
of bottlenecks. Annual reports for research projects would ensure a steady
flow of information for use in planning and programming and provide a basis
for evaluating the performance and productivity of the scientists (para.
5.20).
5.17     IARCs are periodically reviewed by independent panels to determine
whether there is need for major change in policy and direction of their
activities. A similar review system would be designed for KARI. An
outside Review Panel periodically called by the YARI Board, would be given
clearly specified terms of reference reflecting areas of concern on which
KARI management may wish to seek counsel. Such panels would include senior
research staff of IARCs, technical experts nominated by donors and
representatives of "client" Ministries (MOA and MOLD) but not exceed 10
members; KARI would provide a secretariat. Government agreement was
obtained at negotiations that an independent review of KARI's research
program would be carried out every five years and that donors would be
consulted with regard to terms of reference and composition of Review
Panels. It would however, be necessary to have more frequent reviews
(every three years) during the first ten years of KARI. Assurances were
obtained at negotiations that a mid-term review of the NARP would be
undertaken by a joint GOK/KARI/donor team no later than September 30, 1990.
This would be preceeded by an independent Review Panel evaluation of KARI's
research strategy and programs. Government assurances were also obtained
that KARI would prepare a Project Completion Report no later than 9 monW'ts
following completion of the first five year phase of the Project.
B. Training, Terms of Employment and Expert Assistance
Training
5.18     Because of the extreme urgency of training in several areas
relating to KARI's management and research support services, KARI would
specify the training needs for in-service training, based on an analysis of
the existing staff and training gaps; KARI would discuss this plan with the
donors and identify funding support for each category of training. KARI
would in particular ensure that an initial briefing of research staff on



- 34 -
NARP and the reorganization of agricultural research would take place
before December 31, 1987. Egerton University College would be primarily
responsible for providing within country in-service training on research
management and related areas; arrangements would be made with appropriate
institutions for other types of in-service training. It is expected that
Egerton University College would be strengthened under a separate USAID-
assisted project which includes twinning arrangements with the University
of Illinois. The USAID, EEC, ODA, Germany, the Netherlands and other
donors would be making significant provisions for in-service training.
KARI would therefore be expected to have sufficient funds to meet the
recurrent costs of the in-service training courses.
5.19     A draft plan for degree-type training has been prepared (Annex I,
Table 2). Each long-term strategic research proposal (paras. 5.23, 5.24)
would include a detailed plan for degree-related and other training of
scientists in specified disciplines. It is estimated that overseas
training at M.Sc. level would require to be arranged for about 180 B.Sc.
holders and at Ph.D. level for about 45 M.Sc. holders (para. 3.07). The
cost of overseas degree-related training of KARI research staff as
envisaged under the Project, would be fully covered by bilateral donor
assistance. A special attempt would be made to accommodate a smooth
reentry of the newly trained scientists and to ensure that they would make
an early and effective contribution to KARI research by arranging for
degree-related research in Kenya and by providing for continuing linkages
with overseas training institutions and trainers after graduation.
Assurances were obtained during negotiations that KARI would at an early
date, further review staff resources and requirements and prepare a revised
degree training plan with special reference to training to be initiated in
1988/89. Submission to IDA of a plan for degree and in-service training of
KARI staff would be a condition of project effectiveness. This plan would
be updated annually by January 1 and discussed with donors during the
regular joint supervisions beginning in 1988 (para. 5.33).
The Scheme of Service and KARI Staffing
5.20     The Scheme of Service for KARI staff (para. 3.16) would, in
addition to salary improvements, include features to ensure effective
personnel management and improved staff motivation. It was agreed during
negotiations that KARI's Board would approve by not later than March 31,
1988 a revised Scheme of Service and terms and conditions of service for
its staff: these would include:
(i)     detailed job descriptions of all posts;
(ii)    minimum entry qualifications for each level with a higher
starting salary for exceptionally qualified scientists
having a good record of research and publications;
(iii)   incentives for outstanding scientists, such as accelerated
promotion, overseas training and sabbaticals for research
at foreign institutions linked with KARI;
(iv)    in addition to annual performance evaluation by line
supervisors, periodic evaluation of the output of
researchers by an independent body of outstanding

(v)     a research career stream to provide for upward mobility of
scientists in research without having to move into
management positions;
(vi)    a method of recruiting scientists directly by KARI and
measures to facilitate staff transfers between KARI,
Kenyan Universities and the extension service; and
(vii)   adequate transitory provisions for the absorption of
present staff under the new Scheme of Service.
Except for senior management positions, staff growth would be deferred
until the staff establishment is determined and approved and staff
reassignment according to the revised center mandates essentially
completed. Approval of KARI's revised staff structure and establishment
would be a condition of project effectiveness. Containing staff numbers
would facilitate KARI to arrive at a more realistic budget allocation for
non-salary research operating costs in relation to total recurrent costs
(salaries are currently estimated to amount to an average of about 73Z
during the Project period).
External Expert Assistance
5.21     External expert assistance in research fields for which adequately
trained manpower is not presently available in Kenya would be provided by
several bilateral donors. Foreign experts would function within the
framework of the KARI management system and would assist Kenyan scientists
in formulating and implementing research programs. Important features of
such assistance under the Project would be:
(i)     emphasis on external expert assistance in an institutional
framework with well-known research institutions from donor
countries or from other African countries providing
expertise to support KARI;
(ii)    close linkages with IARCs;
(iii)   reliance on short-term assistance by means of brief visits
by experts to study bottlenecks or to train staff in
specific areas; and
(iv)    greater emphasis on training by sending Kenyan counterpart
scientists for short-duration work assignments to the
foreign institutions concerned.
Delinking external expert assistance from donor support to a specific
research program could result in a more effective use of available funds
and external expertise. KARI would explore this approach with the donors,
and in any case, ensure availability of scientific staff with the required
training and experience before a program would be submitted for IDA funding
and approval. KARI would at an early date prepare an updated proposal for
external expert assistance and cooperation in training in those research
areas planned to be initiated during the first three Project years. This
plan would be updated and if necessary revised annually by December 31
beginning in 1988; significant changes would be discussed with donors
during the annual joint supervisions.

C. Regional and National Research Programs: Linkages
Organization, Long-term Research Strategies and Annual Programs
5.22     The organization of Research Centers (illustrated in Annex II,
Chart 2) would be structured around sections for research, training and
support services (e.g. maintenance, farm management, finance and accounts,
and administration). The operational and financial management of non-
research and/or commercial activities would be separated from that of
research and the demands of such activities would be procured separately
from the resources allocated to research. At Centers with more than one
major research program, all service functions would be common. The NRCs
and RRCs would be equal in status; they would relate to each other through
the coordinators, the Program and Factor Committees, as well as through the
participation of scientists working in nationally coordinated programs in
the Research Center Committees. Job descriptions of key staff at NRCs and
RRCs are given in Annex II, Appendix 1. Although priorities between major
research programs would be decided at the national level (para. 5.08),
responsibility for the development of long-term proposals in agreed key
areas of research would be with the research program coordinators and
Center Directors.
5.23     Strategic research proposal development for RRCs would be directly
supervised by the Research Center Directors. For national programs, this
would be the coordinators' task who would also be responsible for the
linkages of their programs with other related research including that at
the RRCs. The initial drafting of annual programs of research and of
annual reports would, for national programs, be assigned to the
coordinators. At the RRCs, senior agricultural research officers would
carry this responsibility under the Director's overall supervision.
Because the RRCs main task involves adaptive research to evolve technology
for the farmers of their mandate areas, Center Directors would be
responsible for the regular participation of researchers in monthly
workshops, their involvement in on-farm work and in diagnostic surveys
(para. 5.28, 5.29). Research staff would be stationed only at the
recognized National and Regional Centers with subcenters and testing sites
manned exclusivel^ by technical staff (except for Mariakani). This would
ensure that at all NRCs and RRCs, the required critical mass of scientific
expertise providing for the establishment of well-balanced
interdisciplinary teams, would be available; it would also facilitate the
provision of facilities such as housing, schools and recreation that are
essential to a conducive work environment. It was agreed at negotiations
that the station rationalization would be completed by June 30, 1989.
5.24     Preparation of some of KARI's long-term nationally coordinated and
regional programs for research is well underway (paras. 3.10-3.15). These
would be: (i) finalized before September 30, 1987; (ii) submitted to IDA
for review; and (iii) approved by KARI. Each major comriodity, factor or
disciplinary program as well as each RRC program would under NARP, propose
a long-term plan and strategy as a framework for research. Such proposals
would include:
(i)     a review of past research and achievements;



- 37 -
(ii)    an inventory and diagnosis of priority constraints in the
mandate area(s);
(iii)   a design including a time frame to overcome these
constraints through research;
(iv)    methods of technology dissemination;
(v)     staff training and external expert assistance
requirements; and
(vi)    investment implications.
5.25     A detailed outline for long-term research program proposals is
provided in Annex I, Appendix 1.  KARI's Director would be ultimately
responsible for the preparation of these strategic research proposals
according to the time s_hedule shown in the POA (Annex III). Before
approval by KARI, these long-term strategies would be reviewed by IDA. The
annual research programs would be designed on the basis of the agreed long-
term strategies. In order to fit the forward budget process (para. 5.14),
the scientists at NRCs and RRCs would in consultation with the respective
Committees (paras. 5.11, 5.13), prepare the proposals for future annual
research programs, including proposed budgets for all activities by mid-
June of any given year. The financial year of concern begins July 1 of the
next year but the experimental year starts much earlier (about February 1)
for Research Centers in the Rift, Western and Coastal regions and somewhat
later (around August 1) for Centers located in Central and Eastern
Provinces. Given these differences and recognizing that relatively few
Centers are characterized by the September to July season, a suggested
calendar for annual Center reporting and research programming has been
provided in Annex II, Table 3.
5.26     The reorganized RRCs would apply a systems-oriented research
approach consisting of constraint diagnosis, problem resolution through on-
station experiments and on-farm studies as well as the dissemination of new
knowledge generated in this manner, through the extension service to the
farming community (Annex I). The specific program of each RRC will reflect
the particular problems related to the resource base, the priority crop and
livestock commodities and the dominant production system(s) in the mandate
area (map IBRD 20117). Where appropriate, the RRCs would conduct
cooperative on-farm research with the Research Foundations for coffee and
tea and other privately funded research to jointly improve extension
messages (para. 5.08).
5.27     In all cases there would be a strong socio-economic input to
address the need for economic analysis of existing and potential production
situations, farm management research and the orientation of newly developed
technologies to the farmers' and national economic considerations. Each
RRC would be charged with responsibility for adaptive research to increase
productivity at the farm level in a given agro-ecological zone and for
training of extension staff in a particular administrative mandate area
through regular monthly workshops or special courses. Depending upon the
range and diversity of the area to be served, RRCs may have several
subcenters for technology testing. Detailed knowledge of the area on which



- 38 -
each RRC works is an essential prerequisite for the formulation of the
RRC's research program. In preparation of the drafting of research
strategy proposals, a "status report" would therefore be composed by each
RRC and incorporated in the long-term research proposal (Annex I, Appendix
1). All RRCs would have interdisciplinary sub-programs of research in:
(i)     diagnosis and analysis of production systems;
(ii)    technology adaptation and development;
(iii)   technology integration in crop/livestock production
systems;
(iv)    on-farm research and technology transfer.
The suggested implementation sequence for RRCs is shown in Annex III.
Research/Extension Interaction
5.28     Scientists at RRCs would initially draw on the stock of ex!sting
technology at NRCs and elsewhere and adapt this knowledge to the needs of
farmers in their mandate areas in cooperation with extension staff;
recommendations would evolve from this. In this process, they would also
identify new problems and priorities for the NRCs to focus on. It was
agreed during negotiations that the mandates of (and relationships between)
KARI, MOA, MOLD and the extension service would be improved so that their
respective roles and responsibilities in the various stages of generation
and dissemination of technology would be clearly defined; these improved
linkages would be introduced no later than July 1, 1988. The envisaged
structure would allow for the joint planning of annual work programs in
areas of common interest and facilitate effective coordination at all
levels. Interaction between research and extension would be improved
through the participation of different levels of extension staff in: (i)
formulation of nationally coordinated research programs; and (ii)
programming research at RRCs. Researchers and extension staff would also
jointly conduct diagnostic surveys and monitor the impact of new technology
including farmers' reactions, to obtain feedback.
5.29     In order to ensure effective research/extension linkages and an
efficient transfer of technology to farmers, the following actions would be
taken by KARI before July 1, 1988:
(i)     appointment of a Research Extension Coordinator (REC) at
Headquarters (job description Annex II, Appendix 1);
(ii)    expansion of the joint research-extension diagnostic teams
for on-farm research in a farming systems perspective
already functioning at some Centers, to all RRCs in order
to study socio-economic and other factors which influence
farmers' decisions such as to facilitate more efficient
technology development;
(iii)   instructions to the Directors of all RRCs to ensure that
at least 2 research scientists would participate in each
district's extension pre-workshop field visits and in the
mn,9$l v worlchrbn .



- 39 -
(iv)    request researchers to train the extension staff and to
promote extension feed-back to research to enable a two-
way flow of information;
(v)     give the Research Center Committee at each RRC a mandate
and composition that ensures that the research system
develops technologies which are tailored to farmers' needs
and constraints; and
(vi)    encourage NRCs to organize technical courses conducted by
research specialists for extension Subject Matter
Specialists.
MOA and MOLD would instruct senior extension Subject Matter Specialists to
regularly participate in the national Commodity and Factor Committees. The
Agricultural Information Center would be used more intensively by the
research and extension organizations. For this purpose KARI and extension
managers would jointly prepare an annual action program to be undertaken by
the Center. The monitoring and evaluation units of KARI, MOA and MOLD
would survey the implementation of the research/extension linkage program
and provide regular feedback to their managements. Progress in this area
would be especially reviewed during joint donor Project supervisions (para.
5.33).
5.30     Another area where research needs to interact more closely with
extension relates to the function of women in agriculture. The important
role of women and the gender-related division in terms of farm labor, type
of farm activities and farm-income in Kenya agriculture is generally
recognized. Women continue to be the main family food producers on small
farms but they also move into commercial farm activities (like maize
production, tea and coffee harvest). Many men are engaged in off-farm
occupations and they therefore participate only in major investment
decisions. As second income receivers, women become more and more involved
in local trade and investment planning (such as cattle purchase if they
derive income from milk sales). Under the NARP, research and extension
would pay much greater attention to how they can most effectively address
the requirements of major decision makers on farms. The national extension
service has already taken several successful initiatives (Annex I).
However, although more effective extension approaches are pursued, the
technical messages have remained the same for male-and female-led farms.
Under the proposed Project, an attempt would be made to develop
differentiated messages (such as on crops, activity mixes, tools,
investment priorities etc.); they would be the outcome of specially
designed farming systems research. First, the extension agents and
researchers would be trained to better understand the range of issues
involved and to conduct surveys with relevant questions. The extension
services would then generate more meaningful messages and/or new research
requirements. Such research might identify questions especially important
to women (e.g. the need for varieties reducing weeding labor, improved
threshing techniques, etc.). Currently, 152 of agricultural researchers
are women and an attempt would be made tG increase their input into
adaptive agricultural research; the Research Center Committees would
monitor progress in this area.



- 40 -
Other Linkages
5.31     Research.  The effectiveness of research in KARI would be
facilitated through strong formal and informal ties with other national and
international centers involved in agricultural research (para. 3.19). For
example, cooperation with the University of Nairobi would include post-
graduate thesis projects, shared external expert assistance, joint staff
appointments, contract and joint research as well as workshops. A
KARI/Nairobi University Working Group would prepare a long-term framework
for cooperation. With foreign institutions, collaboration would involve
exchange of genmplasm to build up gene banks, attendance at conferences and
seminars, exchanges of staff, participation in training courses,
acquisition of publications and cuoperative research projects.
5.32     Services.  Agricultural services that are important in increasing
agricultural productivity and with which research should be closely linked
are credit, input supply and marketing. Existing problems (paras. 1.05-
1.07) are being addressed through the Agricultural Sector Adjustment
Program (paras. 1.08, 1.09). Linkages of research with other services
would be strengthened through the composition of KARI's Board (para. 5.01
and Annex II). In the context of the proposed Project, linkages between
research and these other services would also be intensified through the
mechanism of regular fortnightly training sessions, monthly extension
workshops and through the Provincial Agricultural Research and Extension
Advisory Committee meetings. Representatives of these other services,
including agro-business would be invited to attend these meetings such
that: (i) research and extension would be aware of the actual input supply,
credit and marketing situations in designing research programs and in
formulating recommendations for the adoption of new technology; and (ii)
those involved in the supply of credit, inputs and purchase of produce
would be regularly brought up to date with regard to extension strategy,
the farm activity calender and farmers' probable requirements.
D. Donor Coordination
5.33     A strong and purposeful research management system, effective
financial and budgetary procedures and regular consultation are critical to
donor coordination and would be ensured by the new KARI. The donors have
agreed that the programs to be supported by them would fit NARP in terms of
substance and approximate cost and would be reviewed and approved by the
KARI Specialist Committees given agreed phasing in the light of KARI's
overall research strategy and priorities (paras. 5.08, 5.10 and POA, Annex
III). Donors would channel their funds to meet local expenses entirely
through KARI (para 4.07). At negotiations, it was agreed that: (i) KARI's
Annual Work Program and Budget as well as any proposed amendments of the
POA (Annex III), would be provided for review to all donors, normally
before January 31 each year; and (ii) all donors would each year, in
January or February, be invited to participate with IDA in one joint
mission to evaluate the implementation of the overall program including
managerial, technical, financial and procurement aspects. The first joint-
donor review might however, be undertaken earlier to resolve any
outstanding issues. In addition, most individual donors would, once a
year, review specific research programs supported by them.



- 41 -
5.34     While the donors have agreed that there would be one system within
KARI for management information, monitoring and evaluation, it would be the
responsibility of KARI to use donor funds in setting up such an integrated
system. Assurances were obtained during negotiations that by March 31,
1988 KARI would prepare a proposal for the establishment of information
systems, including monitoring and evaluation, and financial and research
management procedures satisfactory to the donors and would introduce these
new procedures by July 1, 1988.
VI. BENEFITS, JUSTIFICATION AND RISKS
A. Project Benefits
Nature of Project Benefits
6.01     The Project is expected to improve significantly the organization
and management of Kenya's agricultural research system, thereby using
-esearch resources more efficiently. Benefits arising from improved focus
of research priorities, higher quality research, and better
research/extension linkages, would show up in the impact of the revitalized
KARI on agricultural production.   The Project would contribute directly to
accelerating agricultural productivity and growth. The proposed Project
would complement activities under the on-going IDA-assisted agricultural
extension project, its proposed follow-on stage, and planned improvements
in other agricultural services (paras. 1.08, 1.09).
6.02     The nature of Project benefits highlights the importance of
adjusting research priorities to reflect evolving needs and relative
economic benefits. This planning capacity would be created during the
first-phase Project. Annex V, Table 1 and 2 summarize the criteria and
guidelines that KARI plans to use in setting and adjusting research
priorities and provides further details about the consistency between
various indicators and the proportion of funds allocated to different
research programs. It is evident from these data that further examination
of relative resource allocations is required (e.g. in case of oil seeds and
cotton). The Project would help establish institutional capacity and a
systematic approach to setting priorities and adjusting research toward
those activities that generate relatively high economic returns as well as
meet other important national objectives (para. 2.10).
Returns to Investment in Agricultural Research
6.03     Considerable evidence from ex-post analysis shows that investment
in agricultural research in both developed and developing countries has
generated high economic rates of return, exceeding 40? in most cases.2   A
recent Bank-organized cross-country comparison of East and West African
countries suggests that similar, but somewhat lower retulrns have been
obtained in many of these countries. This study however, concludes that
2/ Agricultural Research (Sector Policy Paper, World Bank, June, 1981)
I - O/ -4q



- 42 -
returns are sufficiently attractive to justify further investments,
particularly wben coupled with improved extension. In the case of Kenya, a
study on the diffusion of hybrid maize found relatively high returns from
research during the 1960s and early 19708
6.04     An Economic Rate of Return (ERR) calculation for this type of
project with its first phase institutional emphasis, has little meaning.
The technology improvements developed, the rate and extent of their
adoption by farmers, and the productivity and output gains due to their
application cannot be accurately predicted and are likely to vary
significantly among agroecological zones. Moreover, future gains in
productivity would be attributable not merely to the adoption of
technological innovations but to the combined impact of various support
services (input supply, extension, etc.) as well as to that of the general
economic environment. Finally, the Project's support for research in a
large number of commodity and factor programs spread over numerous Research
Centers, poses serious data problems for a comprehensive ex-ante economic
analysis. The Project's monitoring and evaluation system would aim to
eventually develop a reliable data base for analysis of the economic
returns to various commodity and factor programs as a planning tool for
adjusting research priorities between and within commodity and factor
research programs (para. 5.15).
6.05     Notwithstanding the above comments, it can be shown that even
relatively small increases in productivity attributable to the Project,
would be sufficient to generate an ERR of 20X or more. For example,
equating the present value of the recurrent and capital costs of the
Katumani Regional Research Center with the present value of its benefits,
at an opportunity cost of capital of 20?, implies a yield increase for
maize in the mandate area of only 8? over current levels by the tenth year,
assuming maize is priced on an import-parity basis. If maize is priced on
an export-parity basis, the required yield increase of maize over this ten
year period would be about 301. This illustrative example assumes that all
research benefits would accrue to maize, which comprises only about 60Z of
the total cropped area in Katumani's mandate area (Map IBRD 20117). Annex
V, Table 3 provides the main assumptions and further details of this
example.
6.06     A recent review of 128 Bank projects supporting agricultural
research and extension in 10 countries concluded that the more successful
research organizations in the countries studied, displayed a combination of
some of the following characteristics: a strong central organization with
overall responsibility for most aspects of national research and with
funding control to back it up; some degree of autonomy from the routine
bureaucratic structure of the Ministry of Agriculture; good links, despite
the autonomous or semi-autonomous status, to the national planners and
policy makers; service units tither directly part of, or closely related to
the central research organization; deCentralization of regional research;
and active extension participation in translating research results into
recommendations communicable to users.3 Each of these elements is evident
in the redesign of Kenya's agricultural research system. The same review
3/ Strengthening Agricultural Research and Extension. The World Bank
Experience; Operations Evaluation Department, IBRD Report No. 4684,
September 1983) Pp xi, para. 34.



- 43 -
recommended that Bank-supported agricultural research projects: (i)
emphasize manpower training in particular of research program leaders; (ii)
develop the functions of overall research and station management; (iii)
facilitate research on the integretion of crop and livestock activities and
on farm management; and (iv) consider socio-economic, environmental and
political factors in research program design. The proposed Project is
designed to emphasize these elements.
B. Employment and Poverty Impact
6.07     The Project would have only a comparatively small direct
employment impact during the implementation period. Far more important
would be its indirect impact on employment and income generation in rural
Kenya. Through the eventual adoption of improved agricultural production
and farm management practices by smallholders, the Project would lead to
increases in on-farm productivity and farm output of the majority of
Kenya's 2 million farm families. While the potential yield increases for
crops vary considerably according to agro-climate zone, for most crops
there is scope for attaining significant yield increases over the next 15
years, provided there is an improved research and extension system.
Kenya's high population growth rate of nearly 4? p.a., and its limited land
resources will require sustained productivity increases for most crops and
commodities of at least 4Z p.a. The Project would play a critical role in
meeting these targets through the provisi^n of new and improved
technologies. These yield increases would generate substantial additional
employment and income for farmers, including farm women and agricultural
laborers.
6.08     Approximately 70X of the labor force is employed in agriculture.
Given the land tenure situation, income distribution and population
patterns, the Project's indirect employment and income effects would accrue
predominantly to the poor. In addition, increased productivity would
provide lower priced food to the urban population, most of whom also have
low incomes.
C. Budgetary Implications
6.09.    The Project's impact on GOK's budget, although significant
relative to the past and current allocations to agricultural research,
would be manageable. For example, in 1986/87, the proportion of research
expenditures to the total agriculture budget was 10?; in the Project's
first year, this ratio is expected to reach nearly 15?. On an annual basis
and excluding coffee and tea, the Project would be funding research at
about double the level (in real terms) allocated to research in the 1986/87
budget. The research budget would amount to almost 3X of the Agricultural
Gross Domestic Product, which is high by African standards but justified on
the basis of rapid population growth and limited high potential land. It
must also be realized that these budgetary increases reflect: (i) a
significant increase in donor funds for agricultural research, mostly on a
grant basis; (ii) the inclusion of several research-related expenditures
previously not shown under GOK's research budget (e.g. maintenance and
utility charges), or paid directly by donors (e.g. significant training and
exteral expert assistance-related local expenses); and (iii) the



44 -
introduction of the new Scheme of Service for KARI staff. There is also a
serious degree of deferred underfunding in both recurrent and capital items
essential to generate and sustain research activities. Under the new KARI,
all research costs, including grant funds from donors, would be shown in
the budget.
6.10     The Project's financing plan and the high proportion of donor
funds financing non-salary expenditures (including incremental non-salary
operating expenses) minimizes the Project's budgetary burden on GOK during,
the implementation period. For example, Annex IV, Table 11, which
represents the Project's indicative budgetary impact, shows that the
incremental GOK counterpart funding requirements over the Project period
will range from 4 to 40X more than current GOK counterpart funding levels
provided for KARI's agricultural research programs. This wide range is due
largely to the increased GOK funding for implementing the improved Scheme
of Service (para. 5.20). Moreover, of GOK's total counterpart funding,
about 83Z is to cover all project salaries and reiated allowances.
Moreover, most of the donor funds will finance capital expenditures and
will include funds for operating expenses on a declining basis over the
first phase of the Project. Given the donors' long-term commitment to the
Kenya National Agricultural Research Program (para. 2.04), several
bilateral donors would under . planned next phase continue partial funding
of non-salary recurrent costs beyond the end of the first phase. These
measures would help prepare GOK to finance the increased level of research
activity on a sustained basis. Notwithstanding the Project's financing
arrangements, GOK will need to ensure that the size, scope and phasing of
the research programs are adjusted to available budgetary allocations and
that the required level of funding is sustained well beyond the first phase
Project implementation period. In the event that there would be shortfalls
in the budgetary allocations for the Project, KARI would make the necessary
adjustments in accordance with the agreed priorities and phasing of
research programs and activities (paras. 5.08 - 5.10 and POA, Annex III).
D. Environmental Impact
6.11     The Project would help in providing for the needs of Kenya's
growing population while at the same time maintaining or improving the
resource base. The Project places priority on adaptive research, the
development of pest and disease resistant varieties to decrease dependence
on chemicals and soil and water conservation research (paras. 3.10, 3.13,
and 3.15). To the extent that the modernization of Kenyan agriculture
involves increased use of chemical fertilizers and pesticides, research
carried out under the Project would aim at generating techniques which: (i)
involve greater efficiency in application and thus, relative reductions in
doses per unit of land (compared with likely farmer practices in the
absence of sound research and extension guidance); (ii) draw to a greater
extent on integrated pest management techniques decreasing exclusive
dependency on chemicals; and (iii) provide technology for increased
protection against possible health hazards to use.s of agricultural
chemicals and consumers of farm produce. KARI researchers would ensure
that extension staff (and farmers) receive adequate training in the storage
and application of pesticides.



- 45 -
E. Proiect Risks
6.12     The Project would involve four main risks. These relate tot (i)
effectiveness of the reorganized KARI organization and management; (ii)
adequacy of budgetary funding levels and mechanisms; (iii) adherence to
agreed priorities and balance in research programs; and (iv) relevance and
adoption of research findings. These risks and the Project's measures to
minimize their adverse effects are discussed below.
6.13     Given the Project's unavoidable complexity, there is a risk that
the organizational, procurement, management and coordination arrangements
and procedures may not function as intended or may experience a delay in
functioning properly. To minimize this risk, the Project's design
involves: (i) a management information system coupled with Project
monitoring and evaluation (paras. 3.05, 5.15,); (ii) close donor
coordination, joint supervision missions, and involvement of RMESA staff in
order to ensure support of priority research programs (para. 4.10); and
(iii) a mid-term review in order to reassess the effectiveness of the
Project's organizational arrangements and if necessary, L.ake adjustments
(para. 5.17). During preparation, a two-year period was devoted to
securing consensus and commitment at both the highest political and working
levels on the Project's organizational procedures and coordination
structures (paras. 2.04, 5.33).
6.14     There is a risk that the Project's substantial increases in funding
requirements over current levels may exceed actual GOK budgetary
allocations, particularly to sustain research activities following the
Project's first phase implementation period. GOK's constrained financial
situation requires the implementation of a budget rationalization program,
which includes budgetary ceilings imposed on all Ministries. Donor funds,
however, result in increased ceilings for the agency concerned. To reduce
this risk, IDA obtained from GOK at the time of negotiations, assurances
that agreed procedures for approval and financing of research programs would
be followed (para. 4.07).
6.15     Another major risk in any agricultural research project is that
research findings generated may be irrelevant to farmers's needs or
inapplicable under actual on-farm conditions; that research findings may
not, or only inadequately and with undue delay, be transmitted to farmers
and therefore fail to generate the potential benefits as early and fully as
possible; and that a reversal in agricultural policy reforms currently
underway, may prevent the adoption of technological advances, i.e. render
them unprofitable. Another related risk in this Project involves the
balance in research programs - between and within commodity, factor and
disciplinary programs and between national and regional research programs.
Research results cannot be guaranteed, but to minimize these potential
risks, the Project's design includes:
(i)     emphasis on full funding of RRCs;
(ii)    orientation of research towards location-specific farmers'
needs, resources and skills, that have been clearly
identified prior to the formulation of research programs;
(iii)   emphasis on field-testing and adaptation of technology; and



- 46 -
(iv)    increased cooperation and communication of researchers with
extension staff and farmers for more rapid feed-back on
research impact.
The balance in research programs and consistency with sound economic
policies and research management priorities would be addressed through
strengthening:
(i)     KARI's research management and the effectiveness of KARI's
research planning;
(ii)    the Project's Commodity and Factor Coordination Committees
as well as the Specialist and Center Committees;
(iii)   the M & E system and its role in helping to adjust research
priorities in accordance with changes in economic policies,
national priorities and smallholder requirements; and
(iv)    periodic reviews of KARI's programs and strategies by
outside Review Panels.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND CONDITIONS OF EFFECTIVENESS

would be suitable for a Credit on standard terms of SDR 15.5 million
