KENYA
NATIONAL AGP.ICULTURAL RESEARCH PROJECT
AGRICULTURAL RESEARCH AND RELATED AREAS - PAST AND FUTURE
A. Introduction
1.       The current research programs carried out by MOA's SRD, MOLD's VRD
and KARI have been described in detail in earlier ISNAR reports. The broad
proposed mandates of the envisaged National and Regional Research Centers
have been outlined in the NARP Project Preparation Report. It is not
feasible to include this wealth of documentation in the SAR. It is
nevertheless useful to summarize: (i) the background and current
organization of agricultural research in Kenya; (ii) major present research
programs; and (iii) on a selective basis, what is proposed in major areas
of research. This has been attemped below.
B. Background
2.       In the area of crop-related research, there are presently three
categories of research facilities under SRD (Table 1). The National
Agricultural Research Stations have countrywide responsibility for major
crops like maize (at the Kitale station), wheat (Njoro), horticulture
(Thika), integrated agricultural research (dryland farming at Katumoni) and
specialized services (e.g. soil analysis and crop protection at the
National Agricultural Laboratories). Regional Research Stations are
responsible for research specific to their assigned region. These stations
ensure wider testing of new genetic materials and agronomic practices. The
third category comprises sub-stations or test sites operated by national or
regional stations. A significant research effort also continues at KARI,
Muguga with a research program divided in four departments - veterinary
science (see below), animal production, forestry and crops; plant
quarantine to facilitate import and export of seeds following
internationally agreed procedures is also handled by KARI. Research on
livestock consists of animal production and veterinary research under VRD.
There are several discipline-oriented national centers: animal production
(Naivasha); veterinary science (Muguga and Kabete); and range management
(Kiboko and Marsabit); research on pastuies and fodders is handled by
Kitale. There is also a network of regional veterinary stations. Several
livestock research centers provide research-related services to other
divisions in MOLD and to farmers.
3.       In the past, research centers were frequently established without
thorough study and evaluation of those already in existence. Their
mandates are therefore often ill-defined and insufficiently linked. Also,
due to a lack of communication and overall coordination, there is
significant overlap between research programs.



- 49 -
Annex I
Page 2 of 26
Research Management, Reporting and Programming
4.       Agricultural research in Kenya is currently uncoordinated and
management is weak. Planning and budgeting for individual research
projects are done at the stations with little consideration of national
plans and priorities. However, the station manpower allocations and
overall budget ceilings are defined at the national level by MOA and MOLD
without adequate regard for critical research requirements. Senior
agricultural research staff in MOA and MOLD operate as administrators
rather than as managers setting priorities. Station directors also dedicate
far too much time to routine administrative matters. Due to the lack of a
coherent national agricultural research program, donor-supported projects
have often been implemented outside the existing research system,
distorting information on real research costs.
5.       Management and research information is presently handled by manual
procedures resulting in serious delays and a lack of data critical for
timely budgeting and planning; this makes efficient management and cost
control impossible. Adding to these fundamental problems, Government has
purs,ed hiring, promotion and transfer policies that aim to employ all
school leavers with salary improvements mainly on the basis of seniority,
not performance. To advance their career, specialists often have to shift
to a new field (e.g. from maize to cotton) or become administrators. As a
result, agricultural research is staffed inefficiently and researchers have
become ineffective and demoralized.
6.       There is little review of previous research and a lack of
diagnosis of research needs before experiments are started. Research
reports are often not completed before new work begins. Station annual
reports are frequently behind schedule and some stations have not published
their work since 1980. One of the most glaring deficiencies in the present
research system is the total absence of monitoring and evaluation of
research projects to ensure that objectives are being efficiently pursued,
funds properly accounted for, the desired results achieved, and if not, the
project is modified or terminated. Researchers often are not aware of
findings by international organizations nor of progress made at other
stat:ions in Kenya. Institutional interaction is minimal and depends on
personal contacts rather than a systematic diffusion of information. Due
to lack of funds, most libraries have only the international journals ilp to
1976. The information that does exist is not very accessible due to pl,or
reference services.
7.       The present approach to research program formulation has not
recognized the site-specific demands for new technologies by extension,
farmers and private enterprise. New economic trends and changes in food
preferences are in danger of being neglected. There are gaps in research;
for example the importance of animal production to the small farmer and its
relationship to other components of the farming enterprise has not received
adequate attention. Allocated funds and resources (including donor
support) are spread thinly over a large number of projects some of which



- 50 -
Annex I
Page 3 of 26
are unrelated to priority needs and many lack the critical mass to generate
the desired results. A recent Bank-supported study of the allocation of
manpower resources in relation to commodity values indicated that crops
like maize, sugar and potatoes received a reasonable share of research
attention but that for crops such as rice, wheat, barley and cotton, the
relative allocation of research resources may have be'n too high. However,
roots and tubers gained little research attention. Thus, although other
factors should also be considered in allocating funding and manpower, the
"congruency" of research resources applied in relation to their relative
economic importance could be significantly improved.
8.       For the above reasons, although the system has increased
considerably in size and complexity, research output has since the mid-
1970s, declined in value and quality. It has been unable to effectively
respond to the challenges brought about by increasing population pressure
on land, greater demands for food and raw materials and the predominance of
small holdings. To turn this situation around requires improvements in
agricultural research management, organization, administration and priority
setting and above all, a renewed commitment and dedication of scientific
staff to the purposes of the national agricultural research system.
Financial Support
9.       The total annual budget allocation for research to the earlier
Ministry of Agricultural and Livestock Development and KARI (including
grants by donors) has recently (86/87) been about 13 million Kenyan Pounds
or US$ 15 million equivalent. When NARP was prepared, there were about 40
free-standing donor-assisted research projects involving total financing
(over several years) of approximately US$60 million equivalent. More than
10 different donors were involved and significant technical assistance was
included. The part GOK budget for research amounts to almost 2Z of the
Agricultural Gross Domestic Product which is more than in many other
countries in Africa. However, so much is necessary to cover salaries
(almost 80Z), that too little remains for the actual execution of research.
This situation is different for coffee and tea where Commodity Boards
encompassing all private estate and smallholder producers, finance research
through a cess on produce and adequate funding of non-salary operational
costs is ensured (at about 40Z of total recurrent costs). Government hopes
to expand private sector participation in research to cotton, pyrethrum,
sugar and other commodities. A firm policy of cost sharing between the
public and private sector however, remains to be worked out. Research
stations frequently provide other services to the farming community and
private sector (e.g. seeds, plants, seed quality control, soil fertility
analysis); they sometimes sell agricultural and livestock produce, drugs
and vaccines. Such activities need to be clearly separated from research
(i.e. independently managed); their price setting and accounting needs
improvement.



-51 -
Annex I
Page 4 of 26
C. Present Major Research Programs; International Linkages
Crops
10.      Research on food crops in the past was concentrated mostly on
plant breeding and commercial farming practices in the high potential
areas. Major breakthroughs have been the genetic improvement of maize,
wheat and barley for the highlands and the development of short season
maize varieties for the drier marginal areas. Less research has been
carried out on the technologies necessary to improve productivity of the
smallholder farmers, especially intercropping. Research on major food
crops now deals with maize, wheat, barley, beans and other pulses,
horticultural crops, sorghum, millet, potatoes, cassava, and rice. Kenya's
major export crops are coffee, tea, pineapple, flowers and pyrethrum.
Research on export crops has high priority because they play an important
role in earning foreign exchange. Research on coffee and tea is handled by
special Research Foundations outside the SRD system and is well provided
for. Major coffee research achievements include the breeding for
resistance to coffee berry disease and improved c:op management practices
(e.g. pruning). Past successes in tea research ha-ve been the development
of improved nutritional practices and the generation of better
establishment and pruning methods. Research in these areas is well
organized at least in part because producers have a say in the formulation
of research and its financing. However, closer linkages with the regional
centers and the agricultural extension service need to be forged. Sugar
traditionally has been an industrial crop and most estates do their own
agronomic research. A sugar research program has been started at Kibos to
adapt tractorized methods to small farmer, ox-drawn conditions and to
examine the potential for intercropping sugar with grain legumes and maize.
Cotton research (at Kibos and Mwea Tebere) has focused on selection and
breeding, pest control and agronomy. Crop improvement has proceeded mainly
by testing of introductions from elsewhere. Industry (through the Cotton
Seed and Lint Marketing Board) currently plays a (minor) role in funding
this research. Particularly in dryland areas, yields have been stagnant
and inadequate attention was paid to seed multiplication, research on
intercropping systems and appropriate pest control practices.
Livestock
11.      Research on livestock development at Naivasha covers animal
breeding and nutrition; the species are cattle (beef and dairy), sheep,
pigs, and poultry. The development of grade dairy cattle and contributions
by veterinary science that facilitated their widespread introduction in
Kenya, has been a major accomplishment. The focus of research programs at
the National Range Research Station, Kiboko is on range management,
grassland improvement, pastures, dryland cropping systems, and animal
breeding (cattle, sheep and goats). The Kenya Arid Lands Research Station
at Marsabit is involved in arid lands livestock research; its mandate
partly overlaps with that of Kiboko. Kitale has a strong research program
on planted pastures and forages that are however, suitable mainly for
large-scale ley farming systems.



- 52 -
Annex I
Page 5 of 26
Factors and Disciplines
12.      In addition to the major research programs on crops and livestock
commodities, there are in Kenya several research activities that concern
themselves with factors of production (soil and water conservation,
irrigation management, etc.) or with specific aspects of agricultural
production or disciplines (plant protection, agricultural engineering,
etc.). Research on production factors and disciplines is conducted at
several locations. The National Agricultural Research Laboratories at
Kabete initially provided analytical and veterinary services to the whole
agricultural sector, but soon also began research work on crop production.
This Laboratory concentrated on research on land and water resources
including irrigation. Research on soil and water management for dryland
areas is conducted at the Katumani Center. A soils and agricultural
chemistry section at the National Agricultural Laboratories provides
services for plant and soil analysis as well as for foodstuffs and water
quality. The Kenya Soil Survey located at the Laboratory is well
established and provides a good service. Plant protection at the National
Agricultural Laboratories involves an entomology section which provides a
central research and reference service on insect pests; a plant pathology
unit gives back-up support as a plant disease clinic to advise on diseases
and nematodes and on the screening and use of fungicides.
13.      The Veterinary Research Laboratory at Kabete has from the very
outset been involved in diagnosis of livestock diseases and the development
and sale of vaccines to combat them. This main laboratory and its satellite
at Embakasi (Foot and Mouth Disease) still produce more vaccines than any
other laboratory in East and Central Africa. The research program of the
Veterinary Research Division at KARI, Muguga has responsibility for
research and vaccine production for rinderpest, contagious bovine
pleuropneumonia, and contagious caprine pleuropneumonia. It carries the
national mandate for research work on East Coast Fever. There is also a
research program on important helminth diseases, especially tapeworm in
cattle and liver fluke in sheep. Much stronger linkages are required
between these Centers on animal production and health and the adaptive work
at the regional research stations.
Adaptive Research
14.      Research results obtained earlier were often based on experiments
carried out at only one research station. Existing recommendations
therefore frequently lack sufficient site-specificity. Kenya's enormous
diversity in ecological conditions requires considerable complementary
adaptive research to develop appropriate recommendations. Thus, the
improvement of the Regional Research Centers, making each responsible for a
particular agro-ecological zone and its range of socio-economic conditions,
needs to be given priority. This need was most clearly felt in the semi-
a.rid regions and the Katumani Center started an effort to improve the focus
of adaptive research in the early 809. This work was facilitated by
assistance of the Food and Agriculture Organization and by in-service



- 53 -
Annex I
Page 6 of 26
training courses on farming system research organized by the International
Center for Maize and Wheat Research.  An interdisciplinary team of
scientists at Katumani carried out promising research in the areas of
diagnostic studies and the on-farm testing of new technology in a farming
systems context; these activities also facilitated closer interaction
between research and extension. Aspects of this work were recently
supported in part by the IDA-financed Pilot Agricultural Research Project
(Baringo Pilot Semi-arid Areas Credit 962-KE). The pilot project succeeded
in developing methodologies for evaluating the relevance of past research,
priority setting and formulation of annual work programs. It became clear,
however, that the generation of research programs for the development of
technologies that farmers can easily adopt, requires long-term continuous
interaction between researchers, extension workers and farmers as well as
strong management, discipline, close monitoring and also specific training
programs for researchers and extension staff in research program
development.
Relations with International Research Efforts
15.      Several International Agricultural Research Centers contribute
significantly to agricultural research in Kenya. The International
Laboratory for Research on Animal Diseases cooperates in its programs with
the Veterinary Laboratory at KARI, the Kenya Medical Research Institute and
the University of Nairobi. The Laboratory's research on basic immuiaology
is a valuable complement to the work of the national institutes on vector
and drug research; an active collaboration is involved in respect of the
trial introduction of immunization procedures for East Coast Fever. The
International Center for Research on Maize and Wheat operates a regional
program for East Africa based at Nairobi. It runs 3 main research programs
including training; two on cereals (wheat and maize) and the third on
farming systems research. Since 1982, the International Potato Center has
conducted a successful potato germplasm importation, evaluation,
multiplication and distribution program based at the Plant Quarantine
Service, Muguga. The Center cooperates with potato research at Tigoni and
the Faculty of Agriculture of the University of Nairobi. The International
Council for Research in Agroforestry in Nairobi is concerned with the
collection and evaluation of information about current or potential
agroforestry components and practices and with devising field research
methodology and arranging for their testing. The International Center for
Insect Physiology and Ecology on the campus of the University of Nairobi
has facilities at KARI, Muguga and a field station at Mbita Point. The
Center has since its inception maintained close working relationships with
the Veterinary Research Department of KARI and with SRD. Kenya also
participates in two regional research projects and networks which
contribute to the national research effort: (i) the Semi-arid Food Grains
Research and Development Project in which the International Crops Research
Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics cooperates and which is supported by
the Technical and Research Commission of the Organization of African Unity;
and (ii) the planned Maize Research Network for Eastern and Southern Africa
of the Multinational Programming Centre based in Lusaka, Zambia.



- 54 -
Annex I
Page 7 of 26
16.      These cooperative ties with national and regional agricultural
research centers have contributed significantly to the development of more
productive crop varieties in Kenya. However, notwithstanding the
substantial input by international organizations, their overall impact on
Kenya agricultural research has been less than expected. This may be due
in part to the fact that many IARCs use Kenya as a base for their regional
African activities rather than to solely support the national research
system. However, the lack of an effectively organized national research
system and reluctance (due to sensitivity of Kenyan institutions) to fully
utilize services offered by the IARCs probably also played a role.
Stronger linkages with related international and national institutions
could significantly improve the effectiveness of the Kenya agricultural
research system.
D. Related Agricultural Services
Agricultural Extension
17.      The Kenya National Extension Service has been strengthened since
1983 when the Government embarked on the National Extension Project based
on the "Training and Visit" management system (Cr. 1387-KE). The
competence of the front line extension agents has been greatly enhanced
through monthly workshops and regular training sessions which address
current farmers' problems each fortnight. The system is designed to
provide feedback on the perfornance of new technology to the trainers and
to agricultural research.  Supervision has been facilitated by fixed farm
visits of the field extension workers. Extension activities focus on food,
cash and horticultural crops, livestock production and soil and water
management; overall progress is good. This is particularly the case with
maize in high potential areas where recommendations existed which required
virtually no additional purchased inputs and resulted in significant
increases in yields (e.g. time of planting, depth of sowing, spacing of
seeds, timely weeding). Women have been closely involved and are important
beneficiaries of the project; contact with farmers is reported to be for
about 70t with women who have proven responsive and effective in
implementing recommendations. There has been as yet, insufficient feedback
to research on specific problems of women farmers.
18.      It will however, take much more time to improve extension's
capacity to diagnose the reasons why farmers adopt (or reject) a
recommendation and to (jointly with research) further adapt recommendations
to the farmers' resources and preferences. Furthermore, as farmers improve
their practices, new messages are required. These new messages can only be
provided by research and through greater skills within extension. Longer-
term exposure is also needed to make the new approach part of the national
system. The current project ends mid-1988 and preparation of a second
phase began in December, 1986 with implementation to begin in July, 1988.
The Second National Extension Project would ensure that recommendations
squarely address farmers' needs. Specifically, the Phase II Project would



- 55 -
Annex I
Page 8 of 26
include: (i) improved extension management; (ii) better facilities for
staff (transport and housing); (iii) strengthened extension efforts on
animal husbandry, farm management, plant protection and farm mechanization;
(iv) greatly expanded training; and (v) much closer research-extension
linkages.
Seed Sun?jy
19.      All the consecutive activities of a sound seed industry have been
successfully developed in Kenyas from the development of breeder seeds
through the promotion of commercial seed production, seed certification and
seed marketing. Several companies produce seed for Kenyan farmers, four
local companies produce for export only and 12 foreign seed companies have
production interests in Kenya. Seed quality control was successfully
established through the National Seed Quality Control Service. In the
past, this service performed well and ensured constant seed quality, an
essential factor in obtaining farmers confidence. Improved seeds are made
available through a widespread mostly private retail network. The
estimated use of improved seeds is: maize 50Z of planted area (80Z in high
potential areas); wheat 502; and barley 1002. Total sales in 1983/84 were
about 17.500 tons for maize and 7.000 tons for other seeds. A Bank study
on agricultural inputs in Kenya describes the seed supply situation.1
Serious problems are the lack of certain varieties of seed suitable to
particular agroclimatic conditicos and seed shortages in drought years.  A
major effort should be made to generate new varieties adapted to specific
ecozones and cropping systems; this will require the building up of an
adequate gene bank which can more effectively support selection efforts.
20.      The supply of baseseeds is still the main task of research centers
(except for maize), this could be more efficiently done by the commercial
sector. The supply of seeds and planting material by research institutes
could be subcontracted to private growers or seed producers. Since the
quality control and certification of seeds is crucial to successful seed
distribution, adequate funding of the National Seed Quality Control Service
needs to be assured; certification activities should be financed from the
certification fees.
Veterinary Services
21.      Kenya produces about 1,600 million liters of milk and about
120,000 tons of beef annually.   The productivity of the national herd is
low. It is estimated that the average grade cow produces only about 900
liters of milk per annum compared to a potential of more than 2,000 liters.
Over two thirds of the national milk production and a little less than two-
thirds of beef production comes from smallholders. Cattle mortality from
disease in Kenya is high: 102 annually on small farms and 72 on large
farms. In recent years there has been a deterioration in animal disease
1/ Kenya Agricultural Inputs Review; Report No. 5643-KE; June, 1985.



Annex I
Page 9 of 26
control services in Uganda, Sudan, Ethiopia as well as in Kenya. Because
livestock is taken freely across the borders, livestock in Kenya is under
increasing risk of massive reintroductiun of rinderpest and contagious
boviiie pleuropneumonia. The decline in vaccination against foot and mouth
diseL: has increased incidence. In many areas of Kenya, the Tick Control
Prograni has broken down and the incidence of East Coast Fever has increased
with the result that the smallholder livestock industry is placed in
jeopardy. Responsibility for Kenya's Animal Health Services lies with
KOLD's Department of Veterinary Services which has some of the best
technical expertise in Africa. However, its management capability is weak
and its budget has been declining. A related problem is that cost recovery
for animal health services (vaccinations, dips, artificial insemination)
has been grossly inadequate.  To resolve these problems, Government with
the assistance of four donors including IDA, prepared an Animal Health
Services Rehabilitation Project.2 Given the improvements whibh will be
implemented under this project (Credit 1758-KE) during the next ten years,
the availability of sound extension advice on animal husbandry and
nutrition as well as on mixed farming practices to increase overall
productivity is essential. Research in these areas therefore needs to be
urgently intensified.
E. Training and Technical Assistance
A_gricultural Training; Status and Needs
22.      The capacity of an agricultural research system depends to a large
extent upon the quality of training of its scientists. Degree programs in
agriculture are provided by the University of Nairobi through its Faculties
of Agriculture and Veterinary Medicine. The Department of Animal
Production is a constituent department of both Faculties. Egerton
University College at Nakuru also grants degrees in the agricultural
sciences. At Nairobi, student intake for the Bachelors of Science in
Agriculture course has steadily increased from 41 in 1970/71 to about 180
in 1986 and for the Bachelors degree in Veterinary Medicine from about 40
to over 90.   Egerton University College, in its first year of operation
(86/87), enrolled 137 students for five B.Sc. degree programs. The college
has also been intensively involved in conducting in-service training
courses and workshops for scientists and support staff in the Kenya
agricultural research system. Post-graduate programs (mainly M.Sc., some
Ph.D.) are offered in 9 fields by the Faculty of Agriculture (Nairobi) and
in 7 fields by the Faculty of Veterinary Medicine. At present (1987),
about 140 students are registered for postgraduate programs in Agriculture
and 50 in Veterinary Medicine. Despite many bilateral assistance *rojects,
facilities for post-graduate education are inadequate with serious problems
in staffing, equipment and operating funds; this situation severely affects
the quality of scientific manpower available for research.
21 Kenya Animal Health Services Project, Report No. 6367-KE; December 1986.



- 57 -
Annex I
Page 10 of 26
23.      There is an acute shortage of well-qualified scientists in the
agricultural research system.   Table 2 gives the composition of research
personnel and their degrees by research station and tentative estimates of
future requirements. Out of a total of about 470 (1985) research officers,
there are less than 20 Ph.D. and only about 200 M.Sc degree holders. Some
critical discipline areas are particularly ill-served e.g. only 2
M.Sc/Ph.D. in Soil Science, 1 in Agricultural Engineering, 1 in Soil and
Water Engineering and none in Animal Physiology. It is evident that there
is an urgent need to: (i) increase the number of M.Sc and Ph.D. degree
holders; (ii) strengthen the Kenya graduate and post graduate education
system to meet the present and future training needs; and (iii) establish
greater and more diverse in-service training capacity. However, on the
short-term it would not be feasible for Nairobi University to significantly
increase graduate and postgraduate output at acceptable levels of quality.
Technical Assistance
24.      With about 60 foreign experts (1985), technical assistance
currently constitutes a major element of donor-funded research projects in
Kenya. Although a significant input has been provided to Kenya's
agriculture (e.g. in the breeding of maize hybrids and the development of
zero-grazing systems for grade cattle), the benefits have not always been
commensurate with the numbers of staff. There has been a lack of
management of technical assistance and of its purposeful application. The
TA framework has sometimes been used for the training of young scientists
from donor countries. As a result: (i) T.A. personnel often worked
exclusively on their "own" projects without linkage to those engaged in
other related research programs or even the station Directors; and (ii)
there has been insufficient emphasis on identification, early-on, of Kenyan
counterpart scientists for training and on phasing out technical assistance
when Kenyan scientists became available. GOK therefore views technical
assistance with considerable reservation and wishes to judge its utility on
a case by case basis.
F. Future Research Perspectives for Key Research Areas
25.      Priorities for commodity and factor research recognized in the
NARP have been summarized in Table 3. Long-term strategy proposals have
been drafted for: (i) maize; (ii) tickborne diseases; (iii) soil and water
management; and (iv) the regional research program at Katumani. These
proposals are currently being refined and will later be formally approved
by KARI (and IDA). The implications for annual programs of research and
training have also been determined. These programs would be initiated
during the first two project years and they would serve as models for the
preparation of proposals for additional national and regional research
programs. Past and future research in these areas is therefore discussed
in some detail below. Other research activities that would be part of the
research program initiated during the first two years are only treated in
summary fashion; detailed proposals in these other key areas would be
prepared by KARI during project implementation and approved by IDA. An
outline for such long-term research proposals is given in Appendix 1.

Crops; Maize Research at Kitale
26.      Systematic work on maize improvement at Kitale began in 1956 with
the establishment of a breeding program for late maturing maize; a program
for early maturity material was started simultaneously at Katumani.
Additional research efforts for maize were initiated at Embu for the mid-
season highlands and near Mtwapa at the Coast in the mid 19608. The Kitale
program has been the most productive with release of Kitale Synthetic II in
1961 and the high yielding hybrids H632 and H622 in the early seventies. A
1981 hybrid, H625 is the latest tested Kitale product although more
recently in 1986, improved versions of the hybrids H612D, H613D and H614D
were released. These Kitale hybrids have a high production potential when
grown under intensive management with high levels of inputs. They are
extremely tall, late maturing and mainly suited for mechanised, commercial
production systems. During the 1970's, the Embu station developed hybrid
maize varieties H511 and H512 for medium maturity areas but these are not
widely adopted. Insufficient rainfall after pollination occurs often and
this frequently reduces yield. The coastal lowland program released an
imported hybrid (Pioneer X105A) and the Coast Composite in the early
seventies. Earlier maturing maize varieties compatible with lower and
irregular rainfall were released by the Katumani program in the mid-1960s
(Katumani Synthetic II and IV and Composites A and B).
27.      Agronomic research on maize has been concentrated primarily at
Kitale. In the mid-1970s, an ODA-assisted Maize Agronomy Research Project
focussed on the yield effects of planting dates, plant populations, methods
of weed control and the effects of different rates and application methods
of fertilizers. Although much of this work was done with maize as a sole
crop, a good agronomic understanding of maize production factors in the
high potential areas around Kitale was developed. Much less is known with
regard to the most appropriate crop husbandry practices in the more
marginal areas where maize is often grown in mixed cropping systems. With
the subdivision of farms into smaller and smaller units, multiple and mixed
cropping systems have become much more common and the area under sole maize
is decreasing. A considerable gap exists between the experimental yields
obtained from the combination of improved varieties and the package of
agronomic practices developed by Kitale and actual farmers' yields: the
average on station maize yields for example, show potentials of 10 tlha
compared with averages now obtained on smallholder farms of less than 2
t/ha.
28.      Maize is the most important food crop in Kenya and  this will
become increasingly so in the foreseeable future. Increases in yield of
at least 4.0? per annum are called for in Sessional Paper no. 1 of 1986
with the geographic focus to be on the high potential areas. More than for
any other crop, research should help to retain self-sufficiency in this
national food grain. Maize is poorly adapted to dryland conditions
particularly under low levels of management. While it would not be prudent
to restrict maize research to the high potential areas, the long-term
strategy would be to replace or complement maize in semi-arid areas with
more adapted crops like sorghum and millet because of the better overall
performance of these crops in such difficult environments.



- 59 -
Annex I
Page 12 of 26
29.      The revitalized maize research program would be designed in direct
response to Kenya's development plans and food requirements. Based on a
careful assessment of the different agro-ecological zones, the short,
medium and long range perspective of maize research has been developed and
the technological support needed for increased maize production in
different regions defined. This includes an assessment of the types of
maize varieties needed (hybrids, composites, open-pollinated varieties)
with their desired maturities, grain quality characteristics, plant height
and other desirable agronomic properties. It is envisaged that the RRCs
at Kakamega, Katumani, Embu and Mtwapa would play special roles as
cooperating centers in the coordinated maize program to facilitate the
generation of varieties or hybrids suited to their particular areas.
Research programs are being designed on an interdisciplinary basis with
major emphasis on high productivity combined with more efficient use of
high-cost farm inputs. Possibilities for intercropping systems of maize
with short duration grain legumes would also receive priority attention.
30.      The primary objective of the late maturity breeding  program (at
Kitale) would be to substantially increase the maize harvest index. This
would facilitate greatly boosted (grain) yields without significant
increases in total dry matter production, nutrient uptake or soil moisture
requirements. Efforts would be made to reduce lodging by generating
smaller plants with lower ear placement. Greater emphasis would be given
to the development of prolific hybrids because these are more stable in
intercropping systems. An attempt would be made to accelerate the
development of resistances to maize streak, leaf blight, rust, smut and rot
as well as to Striga and stalk borer. In the semi-arid areas (Katumani),
emphasis would be on further improvement of drought-escaping
characteristics. In the coastal belt (Mtwapa), the development of heat-
tolerant varieties would be particularly important.
31.      Agronomic recommendations require constant and critical re-
evaluation in the different zones taking into account the diversity of the
target groups and changing input costs. The evaluation of production
constraints in the context of the current farming systems of these areas
and the development of suitable technologies to bridge gaps therefore will
be of primary importance in the new maize research program. The focus in
future agronomic work would be on resolving the problems of small-scale
subsistence farmers who market production that is in excess of family
requirements. The main aim of this research would be to reduce risk and to
maximize profits (not necessarily yields). This would include trials on
efficient use of fertilizers (and organic manures) in different
agroclimatic areas, identification of desirable rhizobiums and the
development of viable systems of inter-and relay-cropping. Much of the
research would involve on-farm experimentation accompanied by careful
economic analysis. A prerequisite for success in these types of studies
will be a detailed characterization of the soils of the maize producing
region such that fertilizer trials can be conducted systematically. Maize
is grown by nearly every Kenyan farmer in a great diversity of agroclimatic
environments. Thus, strong linkages with the network of RRCs would be
essential to an effective national maize research program.



- 60 -
Annex I
Page 13 of 26
Animal Health: Research on Tickborne Diseases at Muguga
32.      The tickborne diseases are limited to ecological zones II - IV
because of the distribution of ticks that are vectors of the four main
diseases: East Coast Fever, Heartwater, Anaplasmosis and Redwater. Of
these four, East Coast Fever is the disease with the greatest economic
impact because grade dairy cattle will die if exposed to the disease, and
Zebu cattle will suffer high calf mortality. Dipping programs to eradicate
the vector are very costly and threatened by acaride tolerance build-up in
the tick population (e.g. Boophilus tick). About 24Z of the agricultural
production at farm gate value are affected, including 80% of the cattle
herd. About 60% of Kenyan farming families, especially smallholders suffer
losses due to dipping costs and/or death rates. The state of knowledge for
developing a combined vaccine against all four tickborne diseases is
favorable. ODA-assisted research has developed a methodology to immunize
cattle against East Coast Fever, but at too high a cost to be economical.
Also, cattle immunized against East Coast Fever have frequently died from
Heartwater. Unfortunately, little, if any research results may be
transferred from elsewhere, because East Coast Fever is a localized disease
in Eastern Africa. The International Centers work on the disease, but, so
far without applicable results. The time horizon for reaching simple,
extendable breakthroughs cannot be anticipated but existing results are
encouraging.
33.      The economTc impact of a quadrivalent vaccine against tick-borne
diseases would make the dipping program less critical and save many
animals.   Existing research facilities and research support staff are an
excellent basis for the research. The National Veterinary Research Center,
Muguga has a reputation for its past research on East Coast Fever. If an
effective and economical vaccine could be developed, the network of
government and private animal health services that is being improved under
the Animal Health Services Project (Cr. 1758-KE), could be utilized for its
application. Livestock producers would, no doubt, make use of such a
vaccine. The program therefore would be of the highest priority. The
proposed project to develop a national tick-borne disease research program
for Kenya would be co-ordinated from the National Veterinary Research
Center, Muguga. The program would aim at developing new research
interventions and the adoption of already existing technologies to
counteract the heavy losses in livestock and livestock products resulting
from the major tick-borne diseases. Technology generated through research
would culminate in the development of a new diagonostic test against
Heartwater, better and improved sero-diagnostic tests for Anaplasmosis,
Babesiosis and Theileriosis, which would be cheap, specific and useful for
field studies. The program would also develop or adapt suitable
immunization methods against Anaplasmosis, Babesiosis and Heartwater.
34.      A major effort of this proposed program would be directed at the
application of an infection and treatment method of immunization against
Theileriosis on a countrywide scale, but specifically aimed at helping the
small-scale dairy farmers. Towards the end of the research program,
investigations would be undertaken into having a combined vaccine against



- 61 -
Annex I
Page 14 of 26
the four diseases and this immunization method of control against tick-
borne diseases would be assessed as an alternate method of tick-borne
disease control through cattle dipping. The program would strengthen the
research resources at Muguga and other collaborating centers involved in
tick-borne disease research. There exist excellent interlinkages and
collaboration between Muguga and international institutes, as well as the
local Tick and Wildlife Center at the National Veterinary Research
Laboratory, Kabete. The program to accomplish the goals and objectives for
the mandate of tick-borne diseases is expected to cost approximately 2
million Kenya pounds spread over five years. Methods of monitoring and
evaluating progress of this proposed project have been developed as well as
techniques for reporting and dissemination of the program achievements and
constraints. The success of this program would be reflected by decreased
livestock mortality and increased productivity of the national cattle herd.
The impact would be especially strong in the grade dairy cattle herd
through savings from discontinuation of the dipping program allowing
faster build-up of high-yielding cattle. Such expansion would mean
increased local availability of dairy and beef as well as substantial
savings of foreign exchange. Most of the required manpower would be
derived from local resources although a small component of highly
specialized assistance by foreign experts would be essential.
Factors; Soil and Water Management Research at Katumani
35.      Research focusing on specific subject matter areas andlor factors
of production was previously concentrated mostly at KARI, Muguga (formerly
the EAC) and the National Agricultural Laboratories, Kabete. These
programs would be reorganized as part of the NARP and several activitiee
would be strengthened. In case of shortfalls in available resources,
research should initially be limited to those subjects that are already
ongoing and of high priority. Soil and water management (and also crop
protection) are clearly in this category and comprehensive national
programs would be initiated at an early stage.
36.      Research Centers that are currently working on soil and water
management and conservation and have staff with the background to support
this type of research are: (i) the National Agricultural Laboratories
comprising Soil Testing, Soil Survey and Irrigation and Drainage; (ii) KARI
Muguga, which has departments of Soil Physics, Soil Chemistry, Agro-
meteorology and other supporting disciplines; and (iii) the Katumani
Center. Other centers involved in this subject matter area outside the
Ministry of Agriculture are Departments from the College of Agriculture at
the University of Nairobi as well as some scientific staff at Egerton
University College. Past soil and water conservation and management
research in Kenya has covered a wide range of subjects from erosion
quantification and environment characterization and classification to
mechanical and vegetative runoff and erosion control mithods. Catchment
studies under different types of vegetative cover have confirmed the
importance of canopy. Research on the effects of erosion has established a
significant impact on nutrient availability, moisture holding capacity and



- 62 -
Annex I
Page 15 of 26
o'ierall productivity of soils. Studies on conservation methods and
techniques have primarily addressed the problems encountered in large-scale
farming and most solutions proposed are difficult to implement on
smallholdings without qlaborate provisions for joint action with regard to
establishment and maintenance. Water harvesting potentials have been
investigated for several areas and appear to have promise for some.
37.      In recent years, research for semi-arid areas has concentrated
mostly cn: (i) crop yields and water use in relation to rainfall amounts
and distribution; (ii) the measurement of soil and water losses on cropland
and grazing land under different management practicest (iii) development of
tillage practices (and equipment) for dryland farming; (iv) structural
methods for soil and water conservation on cropland; and (v) testing of
improved cultural practices and farming systems. However, these activities
generally have not yet reached the stage of actual recommendations for the
extension and/or soil conservation service. Much of the available
information is based on case studies and therefore difficult to
extrapolate, many of the reports are not easily accessible. Thus, one of
the priority tasks of a revitalized program will be to inventory and
interpret previous studies and to draw the implications for the current and
future research program for soil and water conservation.
38.      The newly formulated research program on soil and water management
would consist of two main components, one located at Muguga addressing the
problems of high and medium potential areas (agroclimatic zones II and
III), the other at Katumani designed to facilitate soil and water
conservation in arid and semi-arid regions (Zones IV to VII). The program
would be coordinated from Katumani and this Center would closely cooperate
with Regional Research Centers and also the Kiboko National Range Research
Center. Because of a lack of sufficient trained Kenyan manpower in this
area of research, training would be an important aspect; significant
initial assistance through external expertise would be required.
39.      The soil and water management research program would have the
following primary objectives:
(i)     to support a productive agriculture by generating
technology for increased food output that adds to the
quality of rural life;
(ii)    to sustain and where possible improve the productivity of
the land; and
(iii)   to provide a capacity to generate new production
technologies in cooperation with other research programs
and to contribute to training.
Four sub-projects are envisaged, each with several activities:
(i)     a national project with coordinating responsibility;



- 63 -
Annex I
Page 16 of 26
(ii)    a sub-project in semi-arid areas aimed at improved
"conservation farming systems";
(iii)   a sub-project for high potential areas;
(Uv)    a sub-project for arid and semi-arid rangeland (to be
initiated later).
40.      The national project would focus on broad surveys of erosion and
related subjects, national data collection and analysis, establishment of a
data bank, linkages with outside institutions as well as facilitating ties
between research and extension. The subproject for semi-arid areas would
be located at Katumani and have specific responsibility for agroclimatic
zones IV and V. It would focus on establishing relationships between
erosion and soil/climate characteristics, the impact of erosion on
productivity, crop water use and climatic risk and most importantly, on the
development of technically and economically viable conservation farming
systems. The sub-project for high and medium potential areas would
concentrate on the refinement of physical components of soil conservation,
the development of integrated livestock, fodder and food crop production
systems that facilitate erosion control, and improved methods of earth-
moving, ensuring by appropriate research that soil conservation techniques
bring an economic return and can be maintained over the long term. The
(future) arid and semi-arid rangelands project would be designed to
generate improved water infiltration and water harvesting techniques, to
develop management methods for rotational grazing, and arrange for the
collection of fodder shrubs. Its location (possibly Kiboko or Marsabit)
would be determined later; strong linkages would be developed with the
Kenya Rangeland Ecological Monitoring Unit.
Other Nationally Coprdinated Crops Research Programs
41.      Grain Legumes.  The National Horticultural Research Station, Thika
is responsible for iesearch on horticulture, vegetables and grain legumes.
Private enterprise plays an important role in supporting fruits research.
As a group, the grain legumes (i.e. beans, cowpeas, grams and pigeonpeas)
are the second most important category among the foodcrops both with regard
to acreage and production. The yield level of these crops is low due to a
lack of suitable varieties and inadequate information on appropriate
production technology as well as the fact that they are grown primarily in
medium potential areas characterized by harsh conditions. Most research on
beans in the past was done at Thika; some work was carried out at the
University of Nairobi and at Katumani. Several new varieties have been
developed: GLP2 is a good yielder with reasonable resistance to major
diseases particularly in high rainfall areas and GLP4 is a Canadian Wonder
type with good yield potential in areas with medium rainfall. Mwezi Moja
(FLP-1004) is recommended for semi-arid areas. Agronomic research has
studied crop management practices both under sole and intercrop conditions;
areas of research focus have been time of planting, weed control, fertility
management, rhizobium application and crop competiticn effects.



- 64 -
Annex I
Page 17 of 26
42.      Cowpea is grown both for grain and as a leaf vegetable;  the main
production area is Eastern Province but the crop is also grown in Coast and
Western Provinces. Major production constraints are vulnerability to
insect pests and susceptability to fungal and virus diseases. A well-
developed breeding program has started at Katumani; several high yielding
varieties (e.g. Katumani 80 and Machakos 66) have been released and are
grown by farmers; new material has also been developed for dryer areas
(less than 200 mm per season) e.g. KVU 095, and KVU 419. Pigeonpea is the
most important grain legume in semi-arid areas. Research, mostly at
Katumani, has focussed on reconstituting plant type, as well as tolerance
and resistance against insect pests and diseases. Early (120 days), medium
(160-170 days) and late (250 days) maturing groups have been identified and
high yielding lines have been developed in each group. Improved and highly
promising varieties are 60/8 IIRA, 576/3, and E9. At Katumani, work has
also been done on mungbean, dalichos and chickpea; mungbean varieties No.
22 and No. 26 have been particularly sussessful.
43.      Future research for these crops would apply stringent priority
criteria in selecting areas for primary research attention. The two main
research centers to be involved in crop improvement research for legumes
would logically remain Thika and Katumani; the coordinator could be located
at either center. Efforts to develop early maturity, drought-resistant
grain legume varieties of superior and more stable yield will not make a
significant impact unless combined with genetic resistance against major
insect pests. For beans, for example, the focus would be on breeding for
resistance to Bean Common Mosaic and halo blight with agronomic research
being tailored to regional needs, especially thole in the drier
agroclimates. Adaptive agronomic research on grain legumes and the
cropping systems in which they are grown would be conducted at all RRCs
where these crops have an important place in the mandate area.
44.        Wheat.  Although wheat is the third crop in terms of area
harvested, it is surpassed by Irish potatoes, bananas and sugarcane in
addition to maize and beans with regard to value of produiction. Wheat is,
to a great extent, produced by large and medium scale farmers, mainly in
Uasin Gishu and Nakuru districts. The National Plant Breeding Station, at
Njoro is responsible for the breeding and agronomy of wheat, barley,
triticale, oats and oilseeds and also for coordinating research work on
sesame, groundnuts, and soybeans. Research at Njoro in addition covers
other areas of crop protection, soils and nutrition management, and crop
utilization. The station has significant linkages with IARCs engaged in
related research. Njoro has played a major role (with cooperation by
International Centers) in the development of improved wheat variet'es and
better agronomic practices. Significant gaps however, remain between the
station's production technologies and yields versus the average crop yields
and agronomic practices in the main wheat growing areas. The station has
recently attempted to concentrate on developing appropriate technologies
for the production of wheat and other crops by smallholder farmers in the
zone.



- 65 -
Annex I
Page 18 of 26
45.        Demand for wheat in Kenya has been growing at about 7Z per year
as a res"lt of changing eating habits. Wheat production has howaver,
steadily declined during the last decade, reflecting the fragmentation of
large wheat farms and a shift of the better land into maize. The
substantial yield increases achieved through the use of improved
technologies have not compensated for this decline in total production.
Kenya now imports more than a third of its wheat consumption. Prospects
for expansion of wheat area are small. Although in the short to medium
term, agronomic research might help small-scale wheat farmers to produce
more effectively, the magnitude of impact is unlikely to be large.
Research in the longer term might be aimed at expanding and promoting
triticale into drier areas but the dividends of such work are unlikely to
be substantial and/or long lasting. Thus, wheat research having arrived at
a crossroads, would require an in-depth review before decisions on the
scope and orientation of future programs are made. Until such time,
current programs would need to be continued on a maintenance basis. The
review would include a perspective plan with regard to the place of wheat
in Kenya's agriculture given the agroclimatic and socio-economic
characteristics of present and potential areas for wheat production.
Conditions in world markets and expected future developments as well as
alternative production possibilities in axeas that now produce wheat would
also need to be considered. A careful assessment would be made of the
wheat varieties presently recommended to Kenyan farmers and their potential
to replace the traditional varieties of low harvest index and longer
maturity; viable strategies to accelerate adoption would need to be
developed. Crop improvement would emphasize stability in performance and
efficient response to improved management and nutrition; control and
management of wheat diseases (e.g. rust) would need to receive priority
attention. A revitalized wheat program would undoubtedly have to
concentrate on agronomic practices specially tailored to the needs of small
farmers with particular attention to sowing dates, cultivation techniques,
balanced fertilizer application and threshing methods. Rather than
treating wheat as a sole crop, the crop would need to be studied as one
component of a cropping system or sequence.
Other Nationally Coordinated Animal Production and Health Programs
46.        Options may exist for a more effective organization of animal
production and health research; these should be carefully reviewed by KARI
at the time long-term strategic proposals are finalized and before going
ahead with any investment program. NARP represents a historic chance of
putting things right and this opportunity should not be lost. In summary,
the options for (re)organizating KARI's national animal production effort
would be the following (in order of preference):

47.        Intensive dairying.  Smallholder dairying with grade dairy
cattle is one of the most successful developments in Kenya over the last 30
years. Over half of Kenya's farmers and pastoralists live in the highland
zone where grade dairy cattle livestock units consist of grade dairy breeds
and their crosses with the Zebu. The technology for milk production with
grade dairy cattle was developed on large-scale dairy farms during colonial
times. Over the last 30 years, the production system was adapted to
smallholdings; the upgrading process is still underway. By 1985, about 53?
of total milk production came from grade dairy cattle, equivalent to about
122 of the farmgate value of Kenya's total agricultural production. A
quantum jump was achieved compared to the previous system of indigenous
Zebu cattle, with the smallholder dairy cow in Kenya now producing about
3/ Not a priority area of research.



- 67 -
Annex I
Page 20 of 26
900 litres of milk per year. However, dairy cows in the developed
countries reach 5-9 times this level and grade dairy cattle offer many
opportunities for further increases in productivity and land use intensity
with zero grazing (fodder being cut and carried to the cattle kept in
houses or compuunds) being the last innovation. Valuable results for
extension were produced by the Dairy Cattle Research Section at Naivasha
supported by the Netherlands.
48.        Fortunately, environmental conditions in the humid and sub-humid
Kenyan highlands have great similarities to those in the temperate zone
dairy countries, and many results from there can be transferred following
adaptive research for the Kenyan smallholder system. Although some
extendable results are coming forward from the research and extension
services, the untapped potential requires a heavy research input for the
short, medium and long term. Quick progress may be expected regarding
fodders, feeding and management, while the results for animal breeding,
health and physiology research would be of a long-term nature. The
delivery system for research is excellent, following the reorganization of
extension and the expansion of the Dairy Development Project into 14
districts. Smallholder dairying in the highlands, therefore merits a
priority research input. Dairy herds exist at all of the Regional Research
Centers in the high potential areas of Kenya, but so far low priority was
given to research on dairy cattle. The national center at Naivasha has
deteriorated from earlier standards but some good research is conducted
with the dairy cattle herd at Muguga (with regional research being done for
Kiambu district). A decision therefore needs to be made on where to locate
the coordinating responsibility.
49.        Pastures and Forages.  An important contribution of the past
research program on pastures and fodders located at Kitale has been the
generation of a large collection of species of grasses, legumes, fodder
trees and shrubs. All these collected and introduced materials have been
tested and are now grown in several ecological zones. The breeding work
with Rhodes grass and Nandi setaria has resulted in the development of
three new cultivars. A superior clone (in terms of dry matter yield, and
mould resistance) was developed by the Napier grass breeding program.
Significant achievements were made in terms of improved agronomic practices
with particular reference to seed rates, methods of pasture establishment,
legume/grass associations and fertilizer requirements. Several studies
were also carried out to determine stocking rates, appropriate grazing
systems, growth rates, grazing behavior of different breeds of livestock,
and milk production from pastures. However, major changes have taken place
in land tenure within the last two decades and the original large-scale
farming system has been transformed into mainly a smallholder system. This
means that planted pastures, suitable for large-scale ley farming systems
are no longer of the same importance while cultivated forage crops used in
zero-grazing systems have become more important because they are required



- 68 -
Anne% I
Page 21 of 26
by smallholders. Simultaneously, cultivated crop agriculture has expanded
in both high potential and semi-arid areas with a consequent reduction in
the area of planted and/or natural pastures available for grazing and an
increase in the availability of crop by-product feeds. Thus, before long-
term strategies are finalized and coordinating roles assigned, a review of
the most suitable future structure of research is required.
50.        Future pasture and fodder research would need to make a careful
(re)assessment of potential fodder resources of Kenya in the different
agro-ecological zones in order to evaluate suitable research strategies.
This would include an evaluation of the potentials for intensive fodder
production on high potential lands with assured rainfall (or irrigation),
as well as those for rainfed lands of medium potential, and for arid lands
subject to frequent and extreme moisture stress. Because of thet (i)
increasingly important role of the semi-arid areas in animal husbandry; and
(ii) the critical role that livestock production plays in the farming
systems of these areas, research should be focussed in particular on the
needs of farmers in regions of medium potential. With a high density of
ruminant livestock in high and medium potential districts, shortage of
adequate feed, particularly in the dry season, is a constraint on achieving
high productivity in grade dairy cattle, (as well as for wool sheep and
dairy goats). The generation and adoption of farming systems that will
conserve natural resources and sustain yields is another priority objective
in these areas. The ongoing intensification of dairying in high potential
areas could increase overall soil fertility and should not endanger the
resource base. However, stocking rates in semi-arid areas are already high
and a major research and development effort is urgently required to provide
drought-resistant forage and acclimatized livestock for these areas. Many
rangelands are overgrazed and although further study is needed, a major
development effort is urgently required to prevent greater deterioration
and this would need research. Improved employment opportunities would come
about with the enrisaged increase in zero-grazing dairy farming systems and
the close integration of animal husbandry and crop production at the small
mixed farm level; this area therefore would also require a major research
effort.
51.        Based on previous evaluations of existing germ plasm and on new
introductions of grasses and legumes, major aspects of pasture and forage
research would be: (i) the introduction of legumes to ensure more balanced
animal nutrition and improved soil fertility; (ii) the identification of
fodder species for stall feeding and grazing pastures; (iii) the
organization of agronomic research to evolve optimum mixtures of grasses
and legumes, sowing dates, harvesting practices, etc; (iv) the development
of fodder conservation practices for lean periods; and (v) the integration
of animal husbandry and crop production within a single farming system.
52.        Even if it were decided to reassign the coordinating role for
animal nutrition research to Muguga, strong cooperative programs at Kitale
and Katumani would need to be continued. Priority programs at Kitale would
bet



- 69 -
Annex I
Page 22 of 26
(i)   to continue to assemble, maintain and evaluate grasses,
legumes and other forage crops suitable for use in the
agro-ecological zones of Western Kenya; and
(ii) to intensify investigations into the most suitable methods
of propagating, managing and utilizing forage grasses and
legumes that can be used for the feeding of grade dairy
cattle and other classes of livestock in zero-grazing
management systems.
53.        At Katumani there would be (in addition) two other priority
programst
(iii) to investigate the management and utilization of the most
suitable grasses and legumes for natural pastures in
dryland farming; and
(iv) to study the most suitable methods of propagating and
utilizing legume bushes that can be browsed and legume
trees that can be lopped in the ery season to provide
livestock feed.
Program (i) would be replicated at Naivasha (grasses and legumes suitable
for natural pastures in ranching areas) and Mtwapa (grasses and legumes
suitable for pastures in the high rainfall coastal strip). Of a lower
priority at Kitale would be the continuation of forage breeding and ley
pasture investigations. Forage breeding investigations would ultimately be
terminated as resources are not adequate for all types of pasture and
forage research and new varieties of forages can be imported from other
breeding centers.
54.        Animal health research. Knbete.  The previous National
Veterinary Laboratories would have the national mandate for diagnostic work
and resarch on: (i) entomology and acaricide evaluation; (ii) poultry
diseases; (iii) Arboviruses (Rift Valley Fever, Blue Tongue disease, etc.)
and other virus diseases; (iv) disease interactions between wild life and
domestic animals; and (v) veterinary epidemiology and economics. The
proposed clearer separation between animal health research (to be carried
out by KARI) and the quality control and diagnosis functions of the MOLD's
Veterinary Department has significant implications for the operations at
Kabete. The Veterinary Investigation Laboratories would stay outside KARI
and facilities at the Veterinary Laboratories, Kabete as well as the Foot
and Mouth Disease Laboratory at Embakasi would also remain with the
Veterinary Department; the same would be the case for the surveillance of
rinderpest; contagious bovine pleuropneumonia, contagious caprine
pleuropuieumonia, foot and mouth diseaue, helminthic diseases and vaccine
production as well as the dip surveillance program.
55.        Programs that would have a research and field services component
include: ticks and ectoparasitology; epidemiology; foot and mouth disease;
bacterial diseases; helminthology; virus disease and trypanosomiasis.



- 70 -
Annex I
Page 23 of 26
Because of: (M) the importance of in particular the studies of economic
impact of diseases and disease control strategies for priority setting in
animal health research; (ii) the continued relevance of the tick and
ectoparasitology research programs; and (iii) the sizeable losses caused by
helminths to cattle, sheep, goats and poultry, an early resolution of the
mandate question is essential. Immediately thereafer, the strengthening of
these important research programs would need to be initiated.
other Nationally Coordinated Research Programs o Factors and Disciplines
56.        Soil fertility and plant nutrition.  Given the lack of location-
specific recommendations for using chemical (and organic) fertilizers, the
initiation of a nationally coordinated program in this area is urgent.
Such a program would need to include a national trial network to: determine
critical limits of nutrients in soils and plants for establishing
fertilizer requirements of crops; study nutrient management through field
experiments involving time, method, rate and source of fertilizer
application; investigate nutrient economy through organic recycling and
introduction of bio-fertilization; characterize and ameliorate soil-based
constraints to crop production; and examine the effects of agro-chemicals,
including fertilizers and pesticides on soil environment and crop
productivity.
57.        Continuity of the current research on fertilizer use which
involved widespread field trials and computerization of results is
essential to provide a better base for fertilizer recommendations. Most of
this work has in the past been organized from the National Agricultural
Laboratories. Concurrently with the design of long-term strategies for
soil fertility research, the Laboratory's mandate (and that of other
Centers involved in similar research) would need to be reviewed and, if
found necessary, updated.
58.        Plant Protection.  Continued and strengthened work on plant
protection is also important. However, before preparing long-term research
proposals, it will be necessary to determine thze exact responbilities of
the different organizations now involved. The relationships between more
basic work being done at Nairobi University and that at KARI Centers would
need to be spelled out. Integrated pest management would be an important
aspect of such a program, as would be inputs of IARCs in the work on
resistance breeding. Because the use of agrochemicals would also be part
of the mandate, collaboration with the Pesticide Control Board set up under
the Pest Control Products Act of 1984 which includes a Specialist Approval
Committee for Agricultural Pesticides, would also be required. This
Committee is charged with the evaluation of residual long-term effects of
agrochemicals, field inspections, monitoring of trading and manufacturing
of chemicals, etc.
Regional Research Programs
59.        Not all improved technology is accepted by the farmers.
Questions that arise ares (i) has the technology really reached the



_ 71 -
Annex I
Page 24 of 26
farmers; (ii) is the technology economically viable; and (iii) is the
technology relevant to the farmer's needs? To specifically address these
questions, the country has been initially divided into eight agroclimatic
zones of major importance. A network of Regional Research Centers would be
assigned responsibilities of focusing on the improvement of the
productivity of the major production systems in the identified agro-
ecological zones. These Centers include 3 that would serve the dual role
of national and regional research. In addition, some of the NRCs will have
small RRC type programs to serve special areas (e.g. Kiboko and Marsabit).
Research at the RRCs would be holistic and systems-oriented with a focus on
the identification, diagnosis and analysis of production problems;
identification, packaging and testing of available technologies; and
adaptation of technologies for increased productivity at the farm level.
60.        RRCs would play a major role in developing and facilitating
interaction between the research and extension services, in the training of
extension personnel, in promoting joint on-farm research and training
activities and in analysis of feedback information from farmers and
transmission of such data to NRC9; these relations are schematically
illustrated in Chart 1. RRC programs would cover assigned administrative
districts and fully complement Government development strategy.  The
program of each RRC would focus on research in the crops and livestock
production systems characteristic of the mandate areas. They would
develop, through field experiments, packages of agronomic practices and
improved management of crops and animals in production systems that would
maximize returns to investments, and would demonstrate these improved
practices to farmers and extension personnel. They would develop effective
systems of scientific communication with National Research Centers and
promote the flow of new and improved materials and technologies for
testing, improvement and incorporation in recommended practices. The
specific program of each RRC would reflect the problems of the resource
base, the priority crop and livestock commodities, and the characteristic
or dominant production systems in the mandate area. In every case there
would be socio-economic inputs to meet the need for economic analysis of
existing and potential production situations and to ensure the orientation
of technologies to farmers' and national economic considerations.
61.        The area of on-farm research is of critical importance to the
proper linkage between research and extension. The objectives,
methodologies, designs and responsibilities for different activities would
be defined as follows:
(i)    On-farm verification experiments are pre-extension adaptive
research trials carried out on farmers' fields representing
agro-climatic conditions different from those of research
stations; they would consist of several treatments and
replications and could be quite complex. The responsibility
for their design, implementation and analysis rests with
research with assistance provided by extension in
identifying suitable farmers and sites. On-farm
verification experiments would be funded by the RRCs and
entail neither cost nor risk to the farmers on whose fields
they are carried out.



- 72 -
Annex I
Page 25 of 26
(ii)   On-farm trials are tests of new technologies successfully
concluded on research stations. They aim at taking account
of farmers' preferences and constraints before extending a
new technology on a large scale. These trials should be of
a very simple design; treatments should be limited to one or
two with the present common practice as a control. No
participating farmer should be required to replicate a trial
and management level of the trials should be in tune with
the farmer's abilities and practices. However, it is
necessary to involve as many farmer-participants as possible
in order to draw valid conclusions. Research should have
the main responsibility (with extension staff participating)
for designing and analysing the trial results while
extension should select participating farmers, assist in
establishing the trials and provide routine supervision.
Evaluation of the results of on-farm trials should be the
joint responsibility of research and extension (with the
involvement of participating farmers).
(iii) Demonstrations concern proven new technologies which have
passed through all the above steps. Responsibility for
their implementation rests entirely with extension staff and
farmers. However, it is important for research staff and
the diagnostic teams to investigate how the new technology
is actually adopted and to identify the constraints which
may hinder full adoption in order to design further
refinements or devise new research proposals.
62.      The Katumani RRC.  Region-specific, adaptive farming systems
research at Katumani was initiated a few years ago and qualified and
experienced staff is now available. Diagnostic surveys have been completed
for Machakos, Kitui, Embu and Meru districts and reports have been prepared.
Technology testing and adoption studies including economic analyses in these
districts are ongoing and research/extension linkages have been
strengthened. The regional mandate of the Katumani Center covers the agro-
ecological zones LW5, UM4 and UM5.  Rainfall amounts, distribution and
duration are the main factors limiting production. The land use pattern is
mixed farming. Frequent crop failures occur and livestock provides
insurance during times of drought. Due to increased population pressure and
because of the fragile nature of the environment, the land resource base is
deteriorating.
63.      Priority objectives of the RRC, Katumani are based on a diagnosis
of the farmers' most urgent needs. They are in conformity with GOK's
objectives in ongoing development programs and were formulated after in-
depth discussions between researchers, extension workers and farmers. The
immediate goals are:
(i)    to test through adaptive and on-farm research, improved
varieties of maize, sorghum, millet, pigeon-pea, cowpea and
cotton;



- 73 -
Annex I
Page 26 of 26
(ii)   to generate agronomic practices for recommended varieties
with particular emphasis on improved management, low
monetary inputs, drought tolerance and intercropping.
(iii)  to identify high yielding pasture and legume forages and
fodder crops and to develop appropriate management methods
to increase feed for animals;
(iv)   to develop more productive, less risky farming systems which
seek to stabilize production through effective combinations
of crop and livestock enterprises making use of conservation
farming techniques to increase in-situ moisture storage and
reduce erosion;
(v)    to undertake economic analyses of existing and improved
farming practices;
(vi)   to effectively communicate new scientific information to
users and other scientists; and
(vii)  to train staff of extension and other services.
64.      Other Regional Programs.  Because of the significant reservoir of
new technology that needs to be further adapted, priority would be given
under NARP to the rehabilitation and strengthening of the RRC network.
Except for Garissa and Kisii, all regional programs would begin to be
implemented during the first two Project years. Some agroclimatic zones in
dry areas are characterized by an exclusive dependence on extensive
livestock production. Because of their special requirements, a subcenter of
the Mtwapa RRC (Mariakani) and two of the NRCs (Kiboko and Marsabit) would
be charged with specific responsibility for these zones.
